U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUEDES, WANDERSON ENEAS
File: AXX-XX0-917 - MIAMI
July 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
McHaffey, David K., ESQUIRE
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 15, 2003. The respondent’s first motion to reopen was filed by his former counsel. The respondent has now raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against him that is compliant with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The number limitations for motions to reopen, found in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii), is waived, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the merits of the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.

2004 WL 2374592 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NOEL AUGUSTUS CAMPBELL A.K.A. NOEL CAMPBELL
File: AXX-XX3-919 - Buffalo
July 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Haroldeen Hartsfield, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carla J. Hengerer
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility
 
We adopt and affirm the May 19, 2003, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility and all other forms of relief from removal. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). During the hearing on September 11, 2002, the respondent was ordered to file with the Immigration Judge an application for relief from removal by November 12, 2002 (Tr. at 5). However, he failed to do so. Accordingly, we agree that the respondent has abandoned his application for relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c).
 
The respondent has raised on appeal a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, resulting in his failure to submit a completed application for relief from removal by the court-imposed deadline of November 12, 2002. He submitted a copy of a complaint which he filed against his former attorney with the New York State Bar, as well as the attorney’s response. Overall, the evidence does not support the respondent’s contention that his failure to file a timely application for relief from removal was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 2374583 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TAMAN, HANY ARAFA EL SAYED MOHAMED
File: AXX-XX7-878 - SAN DIEGO
July 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hamud, Randall B., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael P. Rummel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent accepted a grant of voluntary departure. He later married a United States citizen. He submitted a motion to reopen, claiming ineffective assistance because his counsel failed to withdraw his request for voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that the respondent failed to satisfy the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). On appeal, the respondent offers for the first time evidence that he has filed a complaint against his former counsel for a failure to file a visa petition upon which a motion to reopen could have been based. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal, and notes that the respondent’s complaint against his former counsel was investigated and closed without action.
 
The Ninth Circuit has stated that strict compliance with the Lozada requirements is not necessary where the goals of that decision have been met. See Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003). However, we find that in this case, these goals have not been met. The respondent’s complaint against his former attorney filed with the disciplinary committee is inconsistent with his motion to reopen and affidavit. The respondent originally claimed ineffective assistance during the hearing regarding a failure to withdraw a request for voluntary departure, and on appeal now claims ineffective assistance in post-hearing relief. We find that, in both cases, the respondent has not demonstrated ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not presented clear and convincing evidence indicating a strong likelihood that the marriage is bona fide under the standard set forthat 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(iii)(B). See Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec.253 (2002). The respondent has not demonstrated his prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status. We conclude that the Immigration Judge appropriately denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2374574 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: QUAN LIN
File: AXX XX8 270 - New York
July 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Karen Jaffe, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on August 27, 2002. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before November 25, 2002. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until June 10, 2004. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.
 
The respondent has made several vague statements concerning the level of representation he has received both from a travel agency posing as a law firm, as well as from other attorneys, and asserts that he has been a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Despite these assertions, we note the respondent has not complied with the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). For an untimely motion to receive the benefit of equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance, the alien must exercise due diligence in pursuing the case during the period to be tolled. The final administrative order was issued by the Board in 2002. Inasmuch as the respondent has waited almost 2 years before filing the instant motion, he has not exercised due diligence in pursuing remedies available to him with regards to the alleged ineffective assistance violations.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2374540 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: UGOCHKWU OPARA A.K.A. ELVIS UGOCHUWU OPARA
File: AXX XX9 244 - Houston
July 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bassey Akpaffiong, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Donovan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. Based on a claim of ineffective assistance of prior counsel, the Nigerian respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated June 17, 2004, in which we denied his prior motion to reopen our June 30, 2003, decision. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion, in which the respondent seeks a further opportunity to pursue applications for cancellation of removal and voluntary departure.
 
With his current motion, the respondent has submitted a June 18, 2004, letter in support of his statement that the State Bar of Texas has notified his former counsel of his bar complaint. He asserts that he has complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for further consideration based on his reiterated claim of ineffective assistance of prior counsel.
 
Regarding our denial of the respondent’s prior motion to reopen for cancellation of removal, we find no basis for granting his motion to reconsider. On December 14, 2001, the Immigration Judge, whose decision we summarily affirmed on June 30, 2003, found in part that the respondent had not met the physical presence, good moral character, or exceptional and extremely unusual hardship requirements for cancellation of removal. When we denied the motion to reopen in June 2004, we noted: “Even if the respondent could have produced additional evidence as to his physical presence and good moral character, he did not explain how the Immigration Judge’s finding as to exceptional and extremely unusual hardship would have been different had the respondent not had ineffective assistance of counsel.” Thus, even assuming arguendo that the respondent complied with the Lozada requirements, we find no basis for granting his motion to reconsider this aspect of the prior decision, because he did not show any resulting prejudice from any ineffective assistance of prior counsel.
 
With respect to voluntary departure, however, we find that we previously overlooked an aspect of the respondent’s case. During the proceedings below, the Immigration Judge incorrectly stated that the respondent was ineligible for voluntary departure as an arriving alien (Tr. at 10). If, as in this case, an alien has been physically present in the United States for 1 year immediately preceding the date the Notice to Appear was served, voluntary departure is available to an arriving alien at the conclusion of the proceedings. See section 240B(b)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(1)(A). Compare section 240B(a)(4) of the Act (providing that an arriving alien is not eligible for voluntary departure before the conclusion of removal proceedings). Moreover, the good moral character requirements for cancellation purposes are not identical to those for voluntary departure. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c); see Matter of Arguelles, 22 I&N Dec. 811 (BIA 1999) (regarding voluntary departure at the conclusion of removal proceedings). Our decision summarily affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision was erroneous regarding voluntary departure, and our denial of his motion to reopen did not address whether the respondent was prejudiced by his former counsel’s erroneous concession that as an arriving alien the respondent was ineligible for voluntary departure at the conclusion of removal proceedings (Tr. at 10-11). Under these circumstances, we shall grant the motion to reconsider for the limited purpose of affording the respondent an opportunity to apply for voluntary departure.
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider the Board’s June 17, 2004, decision is denied to the extent that it denied his motion to reopen for cancellation of removal.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reconsider the Board’s June 17, 2004, decision is granted to the extent that it pertains to his potential eligibility for voluntary departure; the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision on his application for voluntary departure.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2374597 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOHANNA LISSET CHAVEZ-AYALA
File: A78 915 984 - El Paso
July 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lorena Larios, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joe J. Tavarez
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion to reopen proceedings in which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the facts mentioned in the Immigration Judge’s decision are erroneous. Specifically, the respondent asserts that she is not 33 years old, but 20 years old. She is a native and citizen of El Salvador, not Nicaragua. Furthermore, the respondent entered the United States with two other individuals, but not with her nephew. Finally, the respondent claims that the Immigration Judge erred in failing to consider her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as she was a victim of fraud by a notary.
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear in the name of Johanna Lisset Chavez-Ayala (A78 915 984) was issued on May 28, 2003, and personally served on the respondent on that date. The NTA also indicates that the respondent is a native and citizen of El Salvador. Furthermore, the Form I-213 sets forth the respondent’s date of birth as November 30, 1983, such that she was 20 years old at the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 12, 2004. The change of venue motion filed in her case indicates that her only family member in the United States is an aunt in California, and sets forth the respondent’s address as 1005 West Cruces Street, Wilmington, California 90744. The hearing notice was sent to the respondent at this address, and she was ordered removed in absentia when she failed to appear for the hearing on August 5, 2003.
 
In her decision dated April 12, 2004, the Immigration Judge refers to the Form I-213, and finds that the respondent is an adult age 33; a native and citizen of Nicaragua; and that she arrived in the United States in the company of her nephew. The Immigration Judge also notes that the change of venue motion filed in the respondent’s case indicates that the respondent would be seeking relief through her United States citizen mother who resides in Los Angeles, California.
 
We find that the Immigration Judge’s decision is indeed based on erroneous facts. However, it appears that the Immigration Judge relied on the Form I-213 that is contained in a record of proceedings that is attached to the respondent’s record of proceedings. Specifically, the record of proceedings regarding respondent Alma Iris Molina-Montenego (A78 915 991) is attached to the respondent’s record of proceeding, although for no apparent reason. The Notice to Appear for Alma Iris Molina-Montenegro indicates that she is a native and citizen of Nicaragua. The NTA was issued on May 28, 2003, and personally served on the respondent on that date. Furthermore, the Form I-213 sets forth the respondent’s date of birth as September 18, 1969, such that she was 34 years old at the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 12, 2004. The change of venue motion filed in her case indicates that her only family member in the United States is her mother in California, and sets forth the respondent’s address as 1005 West Cruces Street, Wilmington, California 90744. The hearing notice was sent to the respondent at this address, and she also was ordered removed in absentia when she failed to appear for the hearing on August 5, 2003.
 
*2 Based on a review of the two records of proceedings regarding the above-named individuals, it appears that the Immigration Judge relied on the wrong record of proceedings and the wrong Form I-213 when she issued her decision. We find that based upon the evidence of record, the proceedings must be remanded to the Immigration Judge for a thorough review of the two records of proceedings, and for the issuance of a new decision.1 Upon preparation of the decision, the Immigration Judge shall issue an order administratively returning the record to the Board. The Immigration Judge shall serve the administrative return order on the respondent and the Department of Homeland Security. The Board will thereafter give the parties an opportunity to submit briefs in accordance with the regulations.
 
ORDER: The records are returned to the Immigration Court for further action as required above.

FOR THE BOARD

The Immigration Judge should further determine whether these two records should remain together.

2004 WL 2374429 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FELIPE CHIMIL BULMARO
File: AXX XX7 157 - Los Angeles
July 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
D. Jade Mundel, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tracy Nguyen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on January 8, 2004, when this Board summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of former counsel. He contends that his former counsel did not properly advise the respondent on the submission of evidence to sufficiently establish extreme hardship to his United States citizen daughter, and further contends that his daughter suffers from severe nosebleeds which require frequent treatment and cauterization. He notes that his former counsel failed to submit medical records, letters from doctors as to the cause, frequency or seriousness of his daughter’s condition, and any other evidence such as letters from school personnel. The respondent contends that these errors and omissions on the part of the former counsel constituted ineffective assistance which contributed to the negative decision in his case.
 
In support of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has submitted an affidavit, and it appears that a complaint was filed with the California State Bar, although the form is not dated and there is no indication when it was filed. There also is no indication that the respondent’s former attorney has been informed of the allegations leveled against her and that she has been given an opportunity to respond. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Consequently, we do not find that the respondent has established ineffective assistance of counsel. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592 (9th Cir. 2004) (alien did not substantially satisfy Lozada because he failed to submit an affidavit and could not prove that he gave former attorney notice of the ineffective assistance allegations or an adequate opportunity to respond). Furthermore, the respondent has failed to submit any medical or other documentation with his motion to support his claim that his daughter would suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship due to her medical condition, if she were to accompany the respondent to Mexico. Therefore, the respondent has failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel or to show prejudice due to his former attorney’s failure to submit additional documentation regarding the respondent’s daughter and the hardship she would suffer.
 
*2 Moreover, pursuant to section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d), an alien who fails to depart following a grant of voluntary departure, and who has been provided written and oral notice of the consequences of remaining in the United States, is statutorily barred from applying for certain forms of discretionary relief absent a showing of exceptional circumstances for failing to depart. See Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996). As the respondent was given written and oral notice of the consequences of failing to depart and failed to comply with the order granting such relief, the respondent is ineligible for the relief sought. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2374434 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAYNAB MOHAMED HASSAN
File: AXX XX5 221 - San Diego
July 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Phillip F. Fishman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia A. Cline
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; change of venue
 
This case was last before us on March 7, 2003, at which time we granted the respondent’s motion to remand to allow her to pursue an application for adjustment of status before the Immigration Judge. Following the Immigration Court’s April 22, 2003, issuance of an in absentia order of removal in the respondent’s case, the respondent filed a motion to reopen which was denied on July 16, 2003. The respondent now appeals that decision, as well as the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to change venue. The appeal will be sustained.
 
The respondent contends that her prior attorney was ineffective. Our review of allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are governed by our analysis in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Matter of Lozada, we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the applicant setting forth in detail the agreement between the applicant and her legal representative and what actions were to be taken on her behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not.
 
Our review of the record reveals that the respondent has met the Lozada standards. The respondent submitted an affidavit setting forth the understanding between her and her attorney and summarizing the facts that led her to elect not to appear for her hearing. See Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider, Reopen, and Rescind In Absentia Order and Motion to Change Venue (“Respondent’s Motion”) at Exh. C. Additionally, not only has the respondent’s former attorney been informed of the charges against her, the attorney has taken it upon herself to report her own ineffective assistance to her state bar association’s office of professional responsibility. See Respondent’s Motion at Exh. D. This satisfies the final two prongs of the Lozada test. We have also previously found that where an alien’s failure to appear for a hearing is due to the ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has demonstrated “exceptional circumstances” for her failure to appear. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). We therefore find that the respondent’s motion to reopen was meritorious and deserving of a favorable exercise of discretion.
 
*2 We turn next to the respondent’s motion to change venue. An Immigration Judge’s discretion to change venue is subject to the existence of good cause for such a change. See Matter of Rahman, 20 I&N Dec. 480, 482 (BIA 1992). Good cause is determined by balancing the factors we have found relevant to the venue issue. See id. at 482-83 (holding that relevant factors include administrative convenience, expeditious treatment of the case, location of witnesses, and cost of transporting witnesses or evidence to a new location). The party moving for a change of venue must show that he would be materially prejudiced if the hearing were conducted in the venue from which he is requesting a change. See id. at 484. The respondent stated the following facts in support of her motion: she has resided in Minnesota since September 1999; the respondent married in January 2001; her husband, now a naturalized United States citizen, filed a visa petition on her behalf; the visa petition has been approved; the expense of travel from Minnesota to San Diego by the respondent and the witnesses in support of her adjustment application would be prohibitive. See Respondent’s Motion. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not opposed the change of venue on appeal. Inasmuch as it appears that the DHS would not be prejudiced by a change in venue, but that the respondent may indeed be prejudiced by a denial of the request, we find that a change of venue would be appropriate. See Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688, 690 (BIA 1988).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to change venue is granted, and venue is changed from the Immigration Court in San Diego, California, to the Immigration Court in Bloomington, Minnesota.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court in Bloomington for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

2004 WL 2374431 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FELIPE ARRONA-ORTEGA MARTINA LOPEZ DE ARRONA
File: A90 560 516 - San Diego
A90 560 517
July 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos A. Batara, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael P. Rummel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondents timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 12, 2003, denying the respondents’ motion to reopen the removal proceedings. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondents’ removal from the United States, following the respondents’ failure to appear for a hearing on May 14, 2002. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that in a decision dated November 13, 1998, an Immigration Judge denied the respondents’ applications for cancellation of removal and voluntary departure. The respondents timely appealed, and on March 20, 2002, the Board remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.1 The San Diego Immigration Court sent the hearing notice to Kevin Tracy, Esquire, who had represented the respondents before the Immigration Judge and on appeal. Neither the respondents nor Mr. Tracy appeared for the hearing on May 14, 2002, and the respondents were ordered removed in absentia.
 
The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has submitted a brief on appeal, and indicates that the lead respondent, Felipe Arrona-Ortega (A90 560 516), was removed from the United States to Mexico on June 6, 2003. DHS submitted a copy of the executed Warrant of Removal/Deportation, Form I-205, dated June 5, 2003. See DHS Appeal Brief, Attachment A. Therefore, the record reflects that the respondent was removed from the United States subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen.2 Consequently, the Board lacks jurisdiction to reopen the lead respondent’s proceedings. See Navarro-Miranda v. Ashcroft, 330 F.3d 672 (5th Cir. 2003).
 
We will now consider respondent Martina Lopez de Arrona’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. The respondents actually filed a memorandum in support of appeal and motion to reopen under Matter of Lozada. This is the respondents’ first attempt to comply with Lozada, as their motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge only argued lack of proper notice. The respondents did submit an affidavit with their motion to reopen. However, it only pertained to the issue of notice and not any alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. On appeal the respondents have submitted a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. They have submitted an affidavit; respondents’ current counsel avers that their former counsel, Kevin Tracy, Esquire, has been notified of the allegations against him; and they indicate that the incident has been reported to the California State Bar. The respondents contend that the in absentia hearing on May 14, 2002, was fundamentally unfair and greatly prejudiced them, and that their chance to present any type of claim in Immigration Court after the Board remand was destroyed.
 
*2 In a decision dated November 13, 1998, the Immigration Judge determined that the lead respondent, Felipe Arrona-Onega, was not statutorily eligible for cancellation of removal for certain non-permanent residents due to his conviction for an aggravated felony. Subsequently, the Board remanded the record for a determination of the effect of the Court’s Trinidad-Aquino decision on the issue of the lead respondent’s removability. See U.S. v. Trinidad-Aquino, 259 F.3d 1140 (9th Cir. 2001) (the offense of driving under the influence is not a crime of violence as that term is defined in the Act, and therefore cannot support a finding of removability). As respondent Martina Lopez de Arrona was charged as an alien present without being admitted, the Immigration Judge considered her application for cancellation of removal, but ultimately determined that the respondents had failed to establish that any of their children would suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if they were returned to Mexico.
 
Based on the actions of the respondents’ former counsel and the respondents’ substantial compliance with Lozada, we find that they established ineffective assistance of counsel. However, we do not find that respondent Martina Lopez de Arrona has suffered any prejudice, particularly in light of the Immigration Judge’s prior denial of her application for cancellation of removal due to lack of exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to any of her children and the absence of any current allegation that respondent’s removal would cause her children to now meet the level of hardship required. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We note that the Board’s remand was based on the Ninth Circuit Court’s decision in U.S. v. Trinidad-Aquino, 259 F.3d 1140 (9th Cir. 2001) (the offense of driving under the influence is not a crime of violence as that term is defined in the Act, and therefore cannot support a finding of removability).

2

We note that the respondent signed the Notice of Appeal on June 5, 2003, but it was not filed with the Immigration Court until June 9, 2003. The Warrant of Removal/Deportation is dated June 5, 2003. As the respondent was removed following the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen, the removal was lawful. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); see also Wiedersperg v. INS, 896 F.2d 1179 (9th Cir. 1990).

2004 WL 2374424 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHURLAND OVID SINGH
File: A36 498 686 - Napanoch
July 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Winston D. Tucker, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility; motion to remand
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s March 5, 2004, decision finding that he abandoned his application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), for failure to timely file and ordering him removed from the United States. During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and/or reconsider.1 The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed, and the motion will be denied.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in not extending the March 1, 2004, deadline set for the submission of the respondent’s application for a waiver under former section 212(c) of the Act because the respondent is eligible to apply for said relief. See Respondent’s Notice of Appeal attachment. In this regard, we note that all documents and applications that are to be considered in a proceeding before an Immigration Judge must be filed with the Immigration Court having administrative control over the record of proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(a) (2004). In addition, the Immigration Judge may set and extend time limits for the filing of applications and related documents and responses thereto, if any. If an application is not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application shall be deemed waived. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c); Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547 (BIA 1992). As a result, we find that the record supports the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent abandoned his application for a section 212(c) waiver by failing to file it in a timely manner. Matter of R-R-, supra.
 
Through his motion to remand, the respondent, through new counsel, claims that he has been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel because his former attorney failed to timely file his application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 1-2. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. Importantly, it also requires that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Aassad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 In the instant case, the respondent has failed to meet these requirements. In particular, the respondent’s motion does not include either an affidavit setting forth the details of the agreement, if any, that was entered into between the respondent and former counsel nor does it include any evidence indicating that former counsel has been given any notice of the respondent’s grievances or the opportunity to respond.2 See Matter of Lozada, supra. In addition, there is no evidence that a complaint has been filed with any appropriate disciplinary authority with respect to any of former counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities toward the respondent. Id. The respondent has, thus, failed to comply with the technical obligations of Lozada.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the motion is denied.

ORDER
On January 30, 2004, the above entitled matter was heard at the Ulster Correctional Facility Master Calendar session. The respondent was represented by counsel, Brian Figeroux. The Immigration Judge then took pleadings and found that alienage and removability were established by clear and convincing evidence. The respondent was then found to be eligible to file for relief under both cancellation of removal, or under Section 212(c) of the Act. Respondent, through counsel elected to file for the 212(c) relief and to forego any relief under Cancellation of Removal. He was then instructed to file his Form I-191 with the Court. The Immigration Judge instructed the respondent and counsel that his completed Form I-191 was to be returned to the Court not later than March 1, 2004, or he would be deported for failure to file his application. He was given an adjournment in which he was to complete and return the necessary documents. As of today’s date, March 5, 2004, the documents have still not been filed with the Immigration Court as instructed. There is no relief available to this respondent other than under 212(c).
 
*3 The necessary documents were not received by the filing deadline. Therefore, it is deemed that the respondent in this case has abandoned his opportunity to file for the requested relief.
 
Wherefore, the issue of removability having been resolved against the respondent, and since he has abandoned all possible relief by failing to file in a timely manner, the following order is entered:
 
IT IS HEREBY ORDERED THAT THE RESPONDENT BE REMOVED FROM THE UNITED STATES, AND DEPORTED TO GUYANA FOR THE REASONS SET FORTH IN THE NOTICE TO APPEAR.
 
JOE D. MILLER
IMMIGRATION JUDGE
March 5, 2004, Napanoch New York

2004 WL 2374439 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUHAMMAD ALTAF
Files: AXX XX2 548 - New York
July 28, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Khagendra Gharti-Chhetry, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 17, 2004, in which she denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on June 7, 2001. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he did not receive notice of the hearing from either the Immigration Court or his former counsel. He claims that he did not learn of the in absentia order until April 27, 2004, when he went to the Department of Motor Vehicles to renew his license. The respondent also indicates that he is still residing at the same address but he never received a call from his former counsel’s office and no notice of hearing was mailed to his address.
 
Initially, we find that proper notice was provided to the respondent. The record reflects that on January 10, 2001, notice of the hearing was mailed to both the respondent and his former attorney of record, Martin G. Vulaj, Esquire. Consequently, the respondent received notice of the hearing in accordance with the Act. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Thus the respondent received written notice of the hearing, but failed to appear on the scheduled date and time. See section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3)(A). The respondent’s former counsel appeared in court on June 7, 2001, and received personal service of a copy of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order dated June 7, 2001.
 
The respondent filed his motion to reopen on May 5, 2004. The respondent failed to file his motion to reopen within 180 days after issuance of the order of deportation, as is required under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act. See Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998) and Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day time limit under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act). However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit has determined that the deadline for motions to reopen deportation proceedings may be equitably tolled to accommodate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 130 (2nd Cir. 2000). An alien must demonstrate not only that the alien’s constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period that the alien seeks to toll. Id. The respondent waited over almost three years to file a motion to reopen these proceedings based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that the respondent failed to exercise due diligence required for equitable tolling. Consequently, the respondent has failed to establish exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear at the hearing on June 7, 2001. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 2374481 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AI HAO JIANG
File: A73 561 616 - New York
July 27, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Yong Men, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
On appeal the respondent raises the issue of ineffective assistance of former counsel for the first time. With his appeal he has submitted a copy of a complaint and his statement filed with the Departmental Disciplinary Committee, Supreme Court, Appellate Division. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, supra. However, the respondent has not submitted any evidence that his former counsel has been informed of the allegations against him, as required by Matter of Lozada, supra. Furthermore, the Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995).
 
The respondent also sought reopening to apply for adjustment of status. However, he did not submit any documents in support of his motion to reopen regarding adjustment of status. On appeal the respondent has submitted an approval notice and an application for adjustment of status.1 An alien is not required to rescind his or her deportation order under section 242B of the Act if he or she is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). Rather, the motion to reopen is only subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3).2 Under these standards, the respondent is precluded from having his motion considered. The record reflects that the respondent received oral warnings in his native language of the consequences of failing to appear at a scheduled hearing.3 Thus he is not eligible to reopen proceedings on this basis. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Moya-Garcia v. Holder, 9th Cir., June 29, 2009
2004 WL 2374486 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN MOYA-GARCIA MARIA GRISELDA RAMIREZ-RIVERA
Files: AXX XX2 309 - San Francisco
AXX-XX2-310
July 27, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Priscilla Wong, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Arrived at a time or place not designated by the Attorney General (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Continuance; cancellation of removal
 
The respondents appeal from an Immigration Judge decision denying them a continuance and denying their application for cancellation of removal. They also argue that they were ineffectively served by previous counsel. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondents appeared before an Immigration Judge, with counsel, on September 25, 2001, at which time a hearing was scheduled to consider their application for cancellation of removal and an application for relief was submitted. When they returned for the individual hearing, 18 months later, they were represented by new counsel who had been retained a week previously and had inadequate time to prepare to argue the case. New counsel requested a continuance to better prepare, but this was denied. The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; see Gonzalez v. INS, 82 F.3d 903, 908 (9th Cir. 1996); Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996). Here, the respondents had 18 months in which to prepare their case and did not file their motion for a continuance until a few days before the scheduled merits hearing (Tr. at 8-9; Exh 5). We find no reason to question the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that good cause has not been shown for the granting of a continuance.
 
A continuance, however, would have been appropriate, and our remanding of the record would now be proper, if the respondents had been ineffectively served by their previous counsel such that they were denied a fair hearing. We cannot find this to be the case. We first note that we agree with the respondents that the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit requires substantial, but not complete, compliance with the requirements for proving ineffective assistance we laid out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Lin v. Ashcroft, 356 F.3d 1027 (9th Cir. 2004).
 
Here, not only have the respondents failed to comply with Lozada, but, more importantly, the record does not support a conclusion that they were ineffectively served by previous counsel. It appears rather that the respondents, themselves, ineffectively pursued their avenues of relief. There is no indication in the record that the respondents had any agreement, before the one with the newest attorney, with anyone to represent them beyond the master calender hearing in September of 2001. Further, although the lead respondent has submitted an affidavit, it does not claim that he attempted to contact his previous attorney in the 18 months between the master hearing and the Immigration Judge’s order, nor does he claim to have had any agreement for representation. The affidavit simply states that there was no further communication with the attorney, that someone informed him of the needed documents 6 months before the hearing, that he was told how to arrange them 2 weeks before the hearing, and, finally, that he contacted the newer attorney who requested the continuance shortly before the individual hearing. For his part, the attorney who appeared in court for the respondents at the master hearing has submitted an affidavit indicating that it was his understanding that he was only to represent the respondents at that hearing.
 
*2 Although the respondent argues that the situation on its face shows ineffective assistance of counsel, this is simply not the case. The evidence presented and the arguments made fall well short of this goal. The respondents’ application was not presented in an effective manner, but it does not appear that the fault lies with previous counsel. First, there is no evidence in the record of any agreement between the respondents and any previous counsel and the lead respondent makes no claim that any such agreement existed. Further, he makes no claim to have attempted to contact previous counsel during the 18 months between the master and individual hearings. Also, it is not made clear why, if he believed he was already being represented by previous counsel, he sought out new representation so close to the date of his hearing. As we find insufficient evidence of any ineffective assistance of counsel, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Jumaev v. Attorney General of U.S., 3rd Cir., January 30, 2006
2004 WL 2374348 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KHAYDAR SUPKHONOVICH JUMAEV
File: A78 275 559 - Philadelphia
July 27, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tatiana S. Aristova, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
This case was last before the Board on February 17, 2004, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal as untimely. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider. The motion will be denied.
 
The procedural history of this case is somewhat convoluted. The Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) served a Notice to Appear on the respondent in Texas. After numerous continuances, the respondent moved to Philadelphia, PA, and his counsel of record filed a motion to transfer venue, which was granted. The order transferring the case indicated that the respondent’s new address would be 10900 Bustleton Ave., Apt. B-96, Philadelphia, PA 19116, apparently based on a bond security form, Form I-305, submitted with the motion to transfer. After several additional continuances in Philadelphia, the respondent moved to New York, and hired new counsel. His new counsel attempted to file a motion to transfer venue, along with corresponding Notices of Entry of Representatives, Forms EOIR-28 and G-28, and proposed orders. However, since the respondent’s new counsel failed to include the correct number of proposed orders, on August 8, 2002, the Immigration Judge refused to file the motion, as well as the corresponding forms and orders, and returned them to the respondent’s new counsel. Therefore, as of August 8, 2002, the respondent’s Philadelphia attorney was still his attorney of record, and she was still receiving notices from the Immigration Judge. The only information of record with respect to the respondent’s address was his Philadelphia address, as evidenced by the order transferring venue from Texas to Philadelphia, PA, and the Form I-305.
 
Thereafter, the respondent’s Philadelphia attorney filed a motion to withdraw because the respondent had retained new counsel. However, the Immigration Judge denied the motion because she failed to establish that she had notified the respondent of the most recent hearing date. The respondent’s Philadelphia attorney then resubmitted her motion to withdraw and attached a copy of a letter she sent to the respondent’s New York attorney, which indicated that she was withdrawing and that the respondent’s next scheduled hearing was April 23, 2003. On April 15, 2002, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s Philadelphia attorney’s motion to withdraw. Since the respondent’s new attorney had not properly filed a notice of appearance or change of address for the respondent, the Immigration Judge served copies of the order granting the withdrawal to the respondent’s former counsel and to respondent’s last known address of record, which was the Philadelphia address. When the respondent failed to appear at the April 23, 2003, hearing, the Immigration Judge entered an order of removal in absentia, and mailed the order on April 24, 2003, to the respondent’s Philadelphia address.
 
*2 Although the respondent does not indicate when he learned of the in absentia order, his affidavit in support of his motion to reopen suggests that he had actual notice soon after it was issued, and that he asked his New York attorney to file a notice of appeal, all before he lost contact with his New York attorney in May of 2003. At some point, it appears that the respondent contacted his former Philadelphia attorney, who, on October 22, 2004, filed a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The file-stamped copy of the motion does not include a Form EOIR-28 or change of address form. On November 7, 2003, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen as untimely, and mailed a copy of the motion to the respondent at his New York address.
 
On January 7, 2004, the respondent’s Philadelphia counsel filed a Notice of Appeal, and a motion to accept the appeal as timely and/or by certification. As a basis for her motion, the respondent’s counsel alleged that the respondent did not receive the Immigration Judge’s most recent order as his mail box was broken, and, in any event, the Immigration Judge should have mailed the order to her as she was his counsel of record.
 
The Board denied the appeal as untimely. In addition, the Board denied the respondent’s request for certification because there was no evidence that she was counsel of record. As the Board observed, the motion to reopen package did not include a Notice of Entry of Appearance (Form EOIR-28), the Immigration Judge had granted the respondent’s Philadelphia counsel’s motion to withdraw, and the record did not include a Notice of Entry of Appearance for any attorney before the Immigration Court. The respondent then filed the instant motion to reconsider.
 
A motion to reconsider requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). On reconsideration, the respondent contends that she did submit a Form EOIR-28 to the Immigration Court, and that the Immigration Court should have served her, not the respondent. As evidence, she points out that the index to the motion to reopen listed a Notice of Appearance, Form EOIR-28, and a change of address form as exhibits. In addition, she attached a copy of the motion to reopen that she claimed to have filed with the Immigration Court. This copy includes a Form EOIR-28 and a change of address form.
 
We have again reviewed the record of proceedings, and do not find any legal arguments or aspects of the case that were overlooked. The file-stamped copy of the motion to reopen did not include a Form EOIR-28 for therespondent’s Philadelphia attorney or a change of address. Nor, moreover, did the record contain a current Form EOIR-28 for any attorney before the Immigration Court. Therefore, the motion to reconsider shall be denied.
 
*3 Although the record did not contain a current Form EOIR-28 for respondent’s Philadelphia counsel, we note that the DHS filed a response to the motion to reopen and served the respondent’s Philadelphia counsel. And the Immigration Court somehow learned of the respondent’s New York address and mailed the order denying his motion to reopen to his New York address. Based on this information, the respondent may be suggesting that the Immigration Court received the Form EOIR-28 and change of address, but misplaced or misfiled it.1 But even if the respondent’s Philadelphia counsel had included the Form EOIR-28, and the Immigration Court therefore improperly served the order, we would not have granted certification because the respondent’s counsel learned that the motion had been denied on December 10, 2003, but unreasonably waited almost a month, until January 7, 2004, to file the Notice of Appeal.
 
Furthermore, assuming that the respondent’s motion to reconsider was granted, and we certified the appeal as timely, we would nevertheless dismiss the appeal on the merits. Section 1003.23(b)(4)(ii), 8 C.F.R., provides that a removal order entered in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal, if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or at any time if the alien demonstrates that he did not receive notice in accordance with section 239.2 Alleged ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute exceptional circumstances, but is not an exception to the 180-day regulatory time limit to file a motion to reopen an in absentia case. Bejar v. Ashcroft, 324 F:3d 127, 131 (3rd Cir. 2003), citing Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998). The respondent’s in absentia order of removal was dated April 23, 2004. Therefore, the respondent had until October 20, 2004, to file the motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. However, the respondent filed his motion to reopen on October 22, 2004, which was 2 days after the 180-day regulatory period had expired. Consequently, even if we had reached the merits, the respondent’s appeal would be dismissed because the Immigration Judge correctly denied the motion to reopen as untimely.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We note, however, that it is unclear how the Form EOIR-28 and change of address form could have been misfiled or misplaced as they were a part of a motion to reopen package, and nothing else appears to be missing from that package.

2

The respondent did not argue that he did not receive notice in accordance with section 239 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229.

2004 WL 2418664 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARSEN KHANDIKIAN
File: AXX XX8 593 - Los Angeles
July 19, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Deborah Karapetian, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 In an order entered on December 7, 2000, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and granted him an additional 30 days’ voluntary departure. In an order entered on June 4, 2001, we vacated that order, reinstated the respondent’s appeal, and remanded the case to afford him the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status, based on his marriage to a United States citizen. On December 15, 2003, we reinstated our December 7, 2000, dismissal of the appeal and grant of a 30-day period of voluntary departure. The respondent had apparently divorced his wife and no longer was eligible for adjustment. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen must state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing if the motion is granted, shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material, and shall not be granted unless it appears that the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. See subsections (a) and (c) of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2
 
The respondent, through counsel, attempts to make several points in his motion, none of which is availing. First, the respondent claims that he was prevented from pursuing his claim for asylum due to ineffective assistance of counsel. However, at the September 18, 1998, hearing, there was considerable discussion concerning the significant discrepancies among the respondent’s various asylum applications, and his counsel (Tr. at 31) informed the Immigration Judge that the application, considered “completely inaccurate,” was prepared based on information provided by the respondent himself. After a recess, the respondent, through counsel, indicated his desire to withdraw his asylum application and apply for voluntary departure. The respondent (Tr. at 33-34) was specifically asked by the Immigration Judge if he was in “complete agreement” with this course of action, and he responded in the affirmative. There is no indication of coercion or other prejudicial action on the part of the respondent’s then-counsel.
 
The respondent, then pro se, filed an appeal on October 5, 1998, from the Immigration Judge’s decision granting him until March 18, 1999, voluntarily to depart. In his appeal, the respondent only referred to an extension of voluntary departure and did not mention asylum. If the respondent feared persecution in his native Armenia, or felt that he had been harmed by ineffective counsel, he had ample opportunity on October 5, 1998, to make such mention on his appeal form.
 
In any event, the respondent’s motion, insofar as it is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, is untimely pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2.
 
*2 The motion, insofar as it is based on changed country conditions in Armenia, will be denied because the respondent has failed to present persuasive evidence that he faces potential harm in that country.
 
The respondent also requests that his case be consolidated with that of his current wife, who allegedly is applying for both asylum and permanent residency in upcoming proceedings. If, as alleged, the respondent has in fact married an Armenian citizen while in this country, her circumstances would presumably be different than his own, and we fail to comprehend how he could claim derivative status based on his wife’s circumstances. Consequently, since this order closes our action in the respondent’s case, we find no administrative advantage to be gained by consolidating his case with that of his alleged wife. If the respondent’s alleged wife is successful in adjusting her own status by a grant of asylum or other means, then she would be free to file a visa petition on his behalf. We see no need to grant a stay of deportation or extension of voluntary departure at this point, however.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 2418664 (BIA)

2004 WL 1739212 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIANA JIMENEZ REVILLA
File: AXX XX4 428 - Houston
July 15, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Donovan
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was ordered removed by an Immigration Judge in an order dated August 9, 2002. This Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from such decision on December 16, 2003. The respondent has filed a motion to “reopen and reconsider,” arguing that her prior counsel was ineffective in that he failed to file a timely appeal brief on her behalf. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion.
 
We find that the respondent has failed to comply with the procedural requirements we set forth in our decision Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), which governs our review of allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
In Matter of Lozada, we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement between the respondent and his legal representative and what actions were to be taken on his behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not.
 
The respondent has submitted a copy of a “Grievance Form” that she allegedly filed with the State Bar of Texas, in which she complains of her representation by her former counsel, Mr. Robert E. Kahn, Esquire. The respondent has informed the State Bar that Mr. Kahn “failed to appeal on time” which has “created a problem” for her “legal status.” See Grievance Form.
 
The respondent has complied with the Lozada requirements only insofar as she may have informed the appropriate disciplinary authorities of her grievance against her former counsel. Importantly, there is no evidence that the respondent has actually informed Mr. Kahn of the allegation she has made against him. Indeed, in her motion, the respondent asserts only that a copy of the complaint “will be served” on Mr. Kahn, not that it has been served on him so that he may have an opportunity to respond to the allegations.
 
*2 In addition, the respondent has failed to set forth, in detail, the agreement she had with Mr. Kahn. Notably, the minimal information the respondent allegedly provided to the State Bar appears to be incorrect. The respondent alleges in her complaint that Mr. Kahn did not file a timely appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision. However, a timely appeal was, in fact, filed on the respondent’s behalf. Instead, it was simply the appeal brief which counsel did not timely file, although he did subsequently attempt to file a “motion to remand” which essentially set forth the reasons why he believed the Immigration Judge’s decision was incorrect.
 
Accordingly, we find that the respondent has failed to comply with the Lozada procedural requirements. Moreover, even if the respondent had complied with the procedural requirements in support of her allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel, we find that the respondent has not adequately demonstrated that Mr. Kahn’s representation caused her any prejudice. It does not appear that the respondent has any relief available to her from removal in these proceedings. During the August 9, 2002, hearing, the respondent’s counsel informed the Immigration Judge that the respondent had an “E visa” application pending before the DHS, and that she should know within 3 or 4 months if such visa was approved (Tr. at 10-11). We note that neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board has any authority over the approval of such visas. In addition, even though almost 2 years have passed since such hearing, the respondent has never informed the Board of the status of her petition before the DHS.
 
In light of the foregoing, the respondent’s motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 1739199 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BRUCE ANTHONY MCDONALD A.K.A. BRUCE MCDONALD
File: A35 771 044 - Buffalo
July 13, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sophie I. Feal, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Denise C. Hochul
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(C), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(C)º - Convicted of firearms or destructive device violation
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B), I&N Act ¿ 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B)º
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
In a decision dated January 7, 2004, and amended on January 8, 2004, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and ineligible for cancellation of removal for having been convicted of an aggravated felony. The respondent’s appeal of that decision will be sustained. His request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2004).
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, was granted lawful permanent resident status on January 14, 1976. On July 8, 1991, the respondent was convicted in the State of New York, Ithaca City Court, of criminal possession of a weapon in the 4th degree in violation of New York Penal Law 265.01. On October 5, 1999, the respondent was also convicted in the same court of criminal sale of marijuana in the 3d degree and of criminal possession of a controlled substance with intent to sell in violation of New York Penal Law sections 221.45 and 220.16(1). Based on these convictions, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable on the charges listed above and pretermitted the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal. See section 240A(a)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(3).
 
On appeal, the respondent provides evidence that his drug-related convictions have been vacated by a New York County Court under Criminal Procedure Law section 440. As correctly noted by the respondent, we have held that a conviction which is vacated under section 440 does not constitute a conviction for immigration purposes (Respondent’s Br. at 7). Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, 22 I&N Dec. 1378 (BIA 2000). Therefore, the respondent would no longer be removable under sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) and 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), and would be statutorily eligible for cancellation of removal.
 
*2 We also agree with the respondent that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, supra had been overturned by United States v. Campbell, 167 F.3d 94 (2d Cir. 1999) (Respondent’s Br. at 7; I.J. at 4-5). That case was specifically addressed in Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz where we found that United States v. Campbell, a case involving a conviction that was vacated under Texas law in the context of sentence enhancements under federal law, did not compel us to go behind the state court judgment in order to assess the reasons for vacating the decision.1 Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, supra.
 
The respondent has requested that we either administratively close proceedings or grant an indefinite continuance because the District Attorney in his criminal case has filed an appeal of the state court’s decision vacating his drug-related convictions. The respondent has also filed a motion to administratively close proceedings on March 4, 2004. In addition to the uncertainty surrounding the final outcome of the criminal charges against the respondent, he also argues that administrative closure or a continuance is appropriate because he must invoke his Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination in any further removal proceedings to avoid incriminating himself in any further criminal proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 9). We assume that the respondent’s reluctance to testify, however, relates to his drug-related charges and not to his other criminal violations and evidence which would relate to his eligibility for cancellation of removal. It is the respondent’s burden to establish eligibility for cancellation of removal and, to the extent he opts to remain silent about any evidence produced by the DHS, an appropriate weight will have to be assigned to the evidence by the fact-finder. We, therefore, will leave the decision whether to grant a continuance to the Immigration Judge’s discretion and will remand proceedings in order to allow the respondent an opportunity to apply for cancellation of removal.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained; his motion to administratively close proceedings is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable under sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) and 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), and pretermitting the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record of proceedings is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this order.

Campbell, the state court in the respondent’s criminal case vacated his convictions based on ineffective assistance of counsel and, therefore, a showing that the “conviction had been improperly obtained,” a distinction which was referenced in the Second Circuit’s decision in Campbell. United States v. Campbell, supra, at 98.

2004 WL 1739176 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAMES WAMITI KANYI
File: AXX XX5 188 - Bloomington
July 9, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Wilson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kevin Lashus
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On June 24, 2004, the Immigration Judge certified this case to the Board after determining that the Board, and not he, had jurisdiction over the matter. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c). The pertinent procedural history is as follows. In a February 2, 2004, decision the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings filed by the respondent. The respondent then filed a motion to reopen with this Board on February 9, 2004, which was denied on March 23, 2004, as number-barred. Thereafter the respondent filed two motions to reopen before the Immigration Judge, in April and May of 2004. The Immigration Judge’s above-noted certification of the case to the Board followed.
 
We initially note that the reasoning of the Board in our March 23, 2004 decision was erroneous, although the outcome was correct. The respondent’s motion was not number-barred as it was the first such motion that was filed before the Board. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c), 1003.23(b)(1). Moreover, the Board did not have jurisdiction over the respondent’s motion since the Immigration Judge’s February 11, 2003, decision was final as the Board had previously dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen that same decision. However, there was no prejudice to either party in the Board addressing the February 9, 2004, motion to reopen since it was untimely, being filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s February 11, 2003, in absentia decision, which was the final order that the respondent sought to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(i). Furthermore, the February 9, 2004, motion was correctly denied as the respondent was seeking to reopen his case for purposes of seeking a new form of relief, adjustment of status. As the record reflects that the respondent received oral notice of the consequences of failing to appear at his hearing, he is not eligible for adjustment of status for a period of 10 years from the Immigration Judge’s February 2003 final order of removal (Exh. 1). See section 240(b)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; see also Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998).
 
For the sake of judicial expediency, and as the ultimate disposition of this case would not vary, we shall assume jurisdiction over the currently pending two motions to reopen, both of which are predicated on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not an exception to the requirement that a respondent file a motion to reopen within 180 days after the issuance of the order of removal. See Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998) and Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day time limit under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act). As the motions at issue were not filed until more than a year after the Immigration Judge’s February 11, 2003, order, both are untimely and reopening is not warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s motions to reopen are denied.
