2004 WL 1739010 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GERARDO HERNANDEZ ELIZABETH PRADO
Files: AXX XX7 178 - Los Angeles
AXX XX7 179
April 28, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Meredith Brown, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Chimarusti
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection ¿both respondentsº
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondents are husband and wife. They have filed the same motion to reopen, noting them both as the respondents in the current action, even though their underlying claims for suspension of deportation were bifurcated by the Immigration Judge and decided in separate opinions. In addition, the respondents’ appeals to the Board were separately decided as well. Therefore, the motion to reopen concerns different prior Board decisions for each of the respondents. Nonetheless, because the current motion to reopen argues the same cause of action, that is that both respondents were subjected to ineffective assistance of counsel and on that basis should have their underlying claims for suspension of deportation reopened, we will address the current motion to reopen for both respondents in one decision. The motion is denied.
 
Based on the attachments submitted with the current motion to reopen, it appears that the male respondent is requesting that the Board reopen its prior decision in his case dated December 17, 1999, and the female respondent is requesting that the Board reopen its prior decision in her case dated July 16, 2003. Initially, we find that the current motion to reopen has been timely filed for both respondents. The female respondent’s motion to reopen was filed within 90 days of the Board decision. In regard to the male respondent, although the motion to reopen was not filed until nearly 4 years after the Board decision in question, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that a motion to reopen an order based on exceptional circumstances can be equitably tolled until the alien becomes aware of the harm resulting from misconduct of his or her prior attorney, or non-attorney, such as an immigration consultant, which is the situation in the respondents’ case. See Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
Before adjudicating the merits of the respondents’ motion to reopen, we further note that, although this motion to reopen is the male respondent’s second motion to reopen its December 1999 decision, we find that the male respondent’s current motion to reopen is not numerically barred. The Ninth Circuit has held that the numerical limitation on motions to reopen may be waived in cases where a petitioner has been defrauded by an individual purporting to provide legal representation. Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237, 1240 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 The respondents’ motion to reopen alleges that they were subjected to notary fraud and received ineffective representation both before the Immigration Judge and the Board. The facts are not in dispute that in the prior immigration proceedings, both before the Immigration Judge and the Board, the respondents were not represented by an attorney, but apparently were following the advise of an “immigration consultant.” It is only in the current action that the respondents have obtained the services of an attorney. The Ninth Circuit has reopened similar cases in which the respondent has been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, that is, ineffective assistance from an attorney. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002) (although this case involved both an” immigration specialist” and attorneys, the Ninth Circuit found that it was the defective representation received from the attorney which constituted ineffective assistance of counsel, requiring reopening and providing the respondent with a new hearing on the issue of suspension of deportation). The Ninth Circuit has held that there is a “crucial difference” between ineffective assistance of counsel and ineffective assistance of an immigration consultant. See Monjaraz-Munoz v. INS, 327 F.3d 892, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). As the Ninth Circuit noted, “courts and agencies have a degree of institutional control over attorneys that they do not have over freelance ‘immigration consultants”’). Id. Moreover, the Ninth Circuit has specifically held that the erroneous advice of an immigration consultant does not constitute an exceptional circumstance requiring reopening of the proceedings. See Singh-Bhathal v. INS, 170 F.3d 943, 946 (9th Cir. 1999).
 
For these reasons, we conclude that the respondents have not demonstrated that they are entitled to having their deportation proceedings reopened, and we enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondents’ motion to reopen is denied.

IN RE: JOSE QUINONES - SEDILLO

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. These proceedings were last before us on February 18, 2004, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent now moves to reopen and reconsider these proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion is denied.
 
At the respondent’s hearing, the Immigration Judge pretermitted the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal when the respondent failed to file his fingerprints as required. The respondent has claimed that his representative failed to inform him in a timely manner of his obligation to file fingerprints for his application for cancellation. The respondent’s second attorney appealed the decision but did not address the ineffective assistance of counsel issues. The respondent’s third attorney has addressed ineffectiveness of counsel, but has failed to file sufficient documentation to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). For example, the respondent, now represented by his third attorney, has not stated whether a complaint was filed with the disciplinary authorities. Second, we see no evidence beyond statements of the third attorney,1 that the respondent informed the first attorney of his complaint. Also, the respondent has submitted a statement which contradicts in part his testimony about whether or not he was told to get his fingerprints taken; it also does not indicate how he was prejudiced. The respondent has not claimed that he was told a falsehood. Cf. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996).
 
We further find that the respondent did not establish that he is prima facie eligible for the relief he seeks. His conviction for committing domestic violence and several convictions for driving while intoxicated may support a finding that the respondent is not eligible in the exercise of discretion. Finally, we note that the respondent was informed of the necessity of departing the United States under the Immigration Judge’s order granting voluntary departure, since failing to leave would result in the denial of a number of kinds of relief. The respondent did not leave. The filing of a motion does not toll the voluntary departure period. Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROSA ELVIA ZAMUNDIO-LOPEZ FERNANDO LEGIS-ZAMUNDIO MARIA DE LOS ANGELES LEGIS-ZAMUNDIO
Files: A76 610 962 - Los Angeles
A76 610 963
A76 610 964

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se1
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal will be dismissed.2 The record reflects that the respondents waived appeal. The summary of the Immigration Judge’s oral decision for each respondent dated January 31, 2000, reflects that they waived appeal. The transcript page 56, line 20, also reflects that the respondents’ waived appeal. However, on August 25, 2000, the respondents attempted to file a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), which was rejected for lack of proof of service. The respondents each resubmitted the Notice of Appeal and argued that their prior counsel did not advise them of the consequences of accepting a grant of voluntary departure in lieu of removal.
 
Upon review, the Board concludes that the respondents’ decision to waive appeal was knowingly and intelligently made. The record reveals that Immigration Judge discussed the requirements and consequences of voluntary departure prior to the completion of proceedings pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a) (Tr. at 50-54). The Immigration Judge briefly continued proceedings while the respondents’ counsel discussed the issue of voluntary departure prior to their waiver of appeal (Tr. at 53). The Board is satisfied that the waiver of appeal was expressly made by the respondents. Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondents’ waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993).
 
In addition, the Board notes that the respondents attempted to make a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondents have not complied with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada,19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, as noted above, review of the record reveals that counsel discussed voluntary departure with his clients. The record before the Board simply does not support a finding that the respondents received ineffective assistance of counsel before the Immigration Judge.
 
Accordingly, for these reasons, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.

2004 WL 1398686 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CLARA FERNANDA MARIN
File: AXX XX3 469 - Miami
April 20, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicolas A. Olano, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s May 5, 2003, decision denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on November 26, 2002. The request for oral argument is denied. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent failed to establish “exceptional circumstances” for her failure to appear at her scheduled removal hearing on November 26, 2002, such that rescission of the in absentia order was warranted. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act. This Board has held that an order of deportation may be rescinded where the alien properly establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel which amounts to “exceptional circumstances” within the meaning of the Act. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (B1A 1996); see also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent in this case failed to appear at her hearing because she sought advice from an immigration consultant, Mr. James Cook, upon receiving the notice of hearing. Mr. Cook apparently had been assisting her with the visa petition her United States citizen husband wished to file on her behalf, and she contends that he advised her to write a letter to the Immigration Court instead of appearing for the hearing. Unlike the cases cited by the respondent in support of her motion, where courts have found that the time limits for filing a motion to reopen may be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel when aliens are defrauded by persons claiming to be attorneys, the respondent has never claimed that Mr. Cook represented himself to be an attorney. Under these circumstances, it was unreasonable for her to disregard the hearing notice, relying instead on the advice of a person who was not, and did not represent himself to be, an attorney. Compare Singh-Bhathal v. INS, 170 F.3d 943, 946-47 (9th Cir. 1999), with Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000), and Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). For this reason, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear at the scheduled hearing. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOEL ALCALA
File: AXX XX6 414 - Los Angeles
April 14, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Victor D. Nieblas, Esquire
*1 In an order dated February 4, 2004, we affirmed, without opinion, an Immigration Judge’s decision on November 19, 2002, to terminate proceedings. The respondent filed a motion to reconsider, alleging that ineffective assistance of counsel prejudiced his case. The motion will be denied.
 
Since the respondent is not specifically alleging that we committed error of law or fact in our order, his motion is construed as one to reopen. The respondent claims that his former counsel, Mr. Burrier, provided ineffective assistance of counsel, including sending an unprepared associate to represent him at the November 19, 2002, hearing, in which the Immigration Judge terminated proceedings.
 
Given the circumstances, it is difficult to envision what Mr. Burrier or his associate could have argued at the hearing to avoid termination of proceedings. During the hearing, the Immigration Judge was informed that the respondent had been removed from the United States on March 18, 2000, pursuant to section 235(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(1). From the date of his removal, the respondent is ineligible to seek admission for 5 years. Section 212(a)(9) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9). An alien who illegally re-enters the United States after having been removed, while under an order of removal, shall be removed from the United States by reinstating the prior order. The alien has no right to a hearing before an Immigration Judge in such circumstances. 8 C.F.R. § 1241.8. See also section 241(a)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(a)(5); Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998).
 
The proceedings were terminated by application of pertinent statutes and regulations, not as the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. Having been previously removed, the respondent had no right to subsequent proceedings, and they were properly terminated. The Immigration Judge had no discretion in the matter. We are aware of no legal argument that could have been presented which would have overridden the Immigration Judge’s obligation to terminate proceedings under the Act. We note that the respondent has filed a complaint with Mr. Burrier’s state bar, regarding the competency of his representation. In the complaint, the respondent alleges that Mr. Burrier charged him over $3500.00 to represent him and assured him that he would get a “green card.” It is unclear whether Mr. Burrier was aware at the time of the respondent’s removal in 2000, but that is a matter beyond our jurisdiction. In view of the narrow focus of the motion, we must conclude that Mr. Burrier did not provide ineffective assistance of counsel in proceedings for which the respondent had no right in the first place.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.

*1 PER CURIAM. On March 1, 2004, the respondent filed this timely motion requesting reconsideration of our decision dated January 30, 2004. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). In our previous decision, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s March 17, 2003, decision denying her request to reopen the proceedings. The respondent requested reopening before the Immigration Judge based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and to enable her to apply for adjustment of status based upon her marriage to a United States citizen. In affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision, we noted that although the respondent presented evidence that a visa petition had been submitted on her behalf, she nevertheless failed to submit clear and convincing evidence indicating a strong likelihood that her marriage is bona fide. See Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). In support of the instant motion to reconsider, the respondent has submitted evidence that she and her husband are expecting the birth of their child in April 2004; she is covered under her husband’s health insurance policy; and she and her husband share joint financial accounts. In light of this evidence, and because the Department of Homeland Security has not opposed the instant motion, we find it appropriate to reopen the proceedings and to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to enable the respondent to apply for adjustment of status. Accordingly, the motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SIMON LEE-LEON
File: AXX XX7 627 - Harlingen
April 14, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew L. Friedman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lessa N. Whatmough
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on September 17, 1992. We then dismissed the respondent’s appeal of a July 10, 1986, decision of an Immigration Judge which found the respondent deportable as charged, denied him voluntary departure, and ordered him deported to Nicaragua. On January 28, 2004, the respondent filed the motion to reopen which is pending. He wants an opportunity to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), and claims that his inability to file sooner was due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
As pointed out by the DHS, the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (a motion to reopen should be filed within 90 days after final administrative order, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later). The respondent seems to suggest that we should apply equitable tolling in his case. However, the respondent does not present any basis for tolling his filing deadline, as he fails to cite to any case in the Fifth Circuit Court of Appeals which would support such an action. See also Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998); cf. Iavorkski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). Additionally, even if equitable tolling applied, the respondent does not meet the requirement that he file his motion with “due diligence.” See Jobe v. INS, 238 F.3d 96 (1st Cir. 2001). We refer to the passage of time since the Board’s last decision. There is also nothing in this case which would justify sua sponte reopening of the proceedings. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999).
 
Finally, the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for a section 212(c) waiver of inadmissibility in event. He was found deportable for entry without inspection, and there is no comparable exclusion ground. See Matter of Hernandez-Casillas, 20 I&N Dec. 262 (A.G. 1991).
 
The motion to reopen will accordingly be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 1398738 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WALID HASSAN TAMAN A.K.A WALTER HASSAN TAMAN
File: A78 330 707 - Buffalo
April 9, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel M. Griebel
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. Sec.237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
Sec. 237 (a)(3)(D) I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(3)(D)º - False claim of United States citizenship
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and Citizen of Egypt, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s March 28, 2003, denial of his motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the appeal and the motion to reopen. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
The respondent claims that he failed to appear at his master calendar hearing on March 14, 2003, because his attorney’s office called him on March 13, 2003, and told him that the hearing had been cancelled due to a scheduling conflict. Respondent’s letter to the Immigration Judge, 3/26/03 (“First Letter”); Respondent’s Affidavit. When the respondent received the in absentia order, he called the court and was told by the clerk to write a letter to the judge with an explanation. The respondent did so, and the Immigration Judge treated the letter as a motion to reopen. On March 28, 2003, the motion was denied. On April 4, 2003, the respondent timely appealed.
 
In addition to his appeal, the respondent filed a second letter with the Immigration Court, explaining that his first letter was not meant to be a motion to reopen and that he had not otherwise filed a motion to reopen. The letter states that “¿the first letterº was not by any means a motion to reopen my proceeding, especially that I know that there are some rules that must be followed before I can file a motion to reopen my case.” Respondent’s letter to the Immigration Judge, April 7, 2003 (“Second Letter”). The Immigration Judge did not respond to the respondent’s second letter.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should not have considered his first letter to be a motion to reopen, especially in light of his second letter which explicitly stated that his first letter was not a motion to reopen. We agree.
 
While it was reasonable for the Immigration Judge to the treat the respondent’s first letter as a motion to reopen, his second letter should have been treated as a motion to reconsider due to the alleged error of fact that his first letter was not a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2) (2003) (stating that “¿aº motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision ….”). Upon reconsideration, the Immigration Judge should have vacated the motion denial since the respondent had not intended the first letter to be a motion. Absent such action, the respondent was barred from filing a proper motion to rescind. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(4)(ii) (allowing an alien ordered removed in absentia in removal proceedings to file only one motion to reopen).
 
*2 Based on the foregoing, we find it appropriate to remand this case to the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s appeal papers as his motion to rescind pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(4)(ii). On remand, the Immigration Judge should consider the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, which is supported by the appropriate documentation,1 as an exceptional circumstance for his failure to appeal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinon.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, an alien is not required to show prejudice in order to rescind an order of deportation entered following a hearing conducted in absentia under section 242B(c)(3) of the Act. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472, 473 n.2 (BIA 1996).

2004 WL 1398750 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERAFIN GONZALEZ GUADALUPE CHAVEZ
File: A95 443 512 - Los Angeles
A95 443 513
April 6, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Monica M. Little
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
In a decision dated April 3, 2003, an Immigration Judge denied the respondents’ motion to reopen alleging that their application for cancellation of removal, previously deemed abandoned by the Immigration Judge on March 5, 2003, had not been timely filed due to a calendaring error by the respondents’ former counsel. The Immigration Judge found that the respondents had failed to meet the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988),1 and, therefore, denied the motion.
 
Pursuant to precedent established by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the procedural requirements established under Matter of Lozada need not be met where “counsel’s ineffective assistance was obvious and undisputed on the face of the record.” Reyes v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1126, 1130 (9th Cir. 2003) (citing Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000)). In the respondents’ motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, which includes a declaration from one of the attorneys handling the respondents’ case, it is clear that the failure to file a timely application for relief was the fault of counsel. The declaration states that the attorney had taken over calendaring for the law firm but due to unfamiliarity with the system, missed the deadline for filing the application for cancellation of removal. As the respondents appear prima facie eligible for the relief they seek, we find their attorney’s failure to file a timely application caused clear prejudice to the respondents and, therefore, will reverse the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003) (“¿p ºrejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” (quoting Castillo-Perez, supra at 527 n.12 (quoting Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999)))).
 
*2 ORDER: The respondents’ appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondents’ motion to reopen is vacated and the record of proceedings is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

Matter of Lozada, supra holds that a motion to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FATIMA MATOS
Files: AXX XX1 601 - New York
April 6, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Claude H. Kleefield, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the Immigration Court failed to take judicial notice of her name change to “Alvarez” based on her marriage to Jose Alavarez, and therefore neither she nor her attorney appeared because they did not receive a notice of hearing under the respondent’s correct name of “Fatima Alvarez.”
 
The Order to Show Cause was personally served on the respondent under her former married name of Fatima Matos. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent deported in absentia on June 1, 1993, and on September 13, 1993, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the proceedings. The respondent did not appeal that decision. On June 3, 2002, the respondent filed a motion to reopen seeking to apply for adjustment of status based on her marriage to a United States citizen. The record reflects that the notice of hearing regarding the motion to reopen was sent by certified mail to the respondent’s attorney, Claude H. Kleefield, Esquire, on August 13, 2002, and was received by her attorney on August 15, 2002. As the respondent’s attorney received notice of the hearing, proper notice was provided to the respondent. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Neither the respondent nor her attorney appeared for the hearing on August 23, 2002, and the respondent was ordered deported in absentia.
 
The respondent’s argument on appeal that she and her attorney failed to appear for the hearing because the notice of hearing was not under the respondent’s newly married name of “Fatima Alvarez” is without merit. Both the certified mail notice and the hearing notice sent to the respondent’s attorney clearly set forth the respondent’s alien registration number. Furthermore, on appeal the respondent states that “the previous motion to re-open which was granted referred to ALVAREZ in all the documents.” However, the previous motion to reopen was not granted by the Immigration Judge. Rather, the Immigration Judge scheduled a hearing on the motion to reopen only. See I.J. Dec. dated August 23, 2002 (stating that the “respondent and her attorney failed to appear at the motion hearing on August 23, 2002”).
 
*2 Finally, we note that the respondent has not made a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 1398644 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CLEVELAND REUBEN FLOWERS
File: AXX XX3 715 - Los Angeles
March 23, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Laurence B. Donoghue, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julie C. Chimarusti,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on January 14, 2004, when we affirmed, without opinion, the results of an Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable as charged, ineligible for relief, and ordering him removed from the United States to Belize. In a motion filed on February 25, 2004, the respondent requests that we reopen proceedings to permit him to apply for relief under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the respondent’s motion. The respondent’s motion is denied, and his request for a stay of removal pending adjudication of this motion is denied as moot.
 
In his motion, the respondent contends that his prior counsel incorrectly advised him that he was ineligible for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act. To the extent the respondent is raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his prior attorney, he has not even attempted to comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9 th Cir. 2000); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000). Moreover, the respondent in his motion has not established that he is in fact eligible for section 212(c) relief. In INS v. St. Cyr, 121 S.Ct 2271 (2001), the Supreme Court held that the restrictions on section 212(c) relief do not apply retroactively to an alien who pled guilty prior to April 24, 1996. The record in this case establishes that the respondent pled guilty on July 29, 1996, for the offense of possession for sale of cocaine base. In addition, the record reflects that the respondent was convicted for the offense of possession of a narcotic controlled substance on April 6, 1998. As these convictions were post-AEDPA, the respondent is ineligible for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act under INS v. St. Cyr, supra.
 
In his motion, the respondent also contends that if he had received a speedy criminal trial with respect to his 1996 conviction and been given the opportunity to enter a plea in a timely manner, he would now be eligible for section 212(c) relief. The record establishes that the respondent pled guilty on July 29, 1996, for the offense of possession for sale of cocaine base, and that the offense was committed in March of 1994. However, this Board does not have jurisdiction in these proceedings to consider the respondent’s constitutional or due process challenges to his criminal proceedings. The respondent was convicted for immigration purposes on July 29, 1996, when he entered his guilty plea. Moreover, even if the respondent’s guilty plea had been entered prior to AEDPA, as set forth above, the respondent is ineligible for section 212(c) relief due to his 1998 possession conviction.
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen to apply for relief under section 212(c) of the Act will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

IN RE: FELICITAS ALMANZA DE ESPINDOLA A.K.A. FELICITAS ESPINDOLA
File: AXX XX7 169 - Los Angeles

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mariela Caravetta, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. On November 16, 2001, following the respondent’s failure to appear, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed to Mexico. On June 20, 2002, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on August 14, 2002. On August 26, 2002, the respondent appealed from that decision, and on August 14, 2003, the Board affirmed the decision of the of the Immigration Judge. On September 3, 2003, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and reconsider with the Board. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not opposed the motion. The motion will be granted.
 
With her motion, the respondent has provided evidence that the Notice to Appear was mailed to a slightly incorrect address, and evidence that DHS had mailed other correspondence to the respondent’s correct address. In the motion, the respondent again argues that exceptional circumstances exist due to a medical disability. In addition, the respondent argues that she did not timely file her motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge because of the ineffective assistance of counsel, and that she is entitled to the tolling of the time limit to file a motion to reopen and a waiver of the numerical limitation for a motion to reopen because she was the victim of fraud and the ineffective assistance of counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent also argues that she is eligible for a waiver under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Naturalization Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c).
 
Upon consideration, we find it appropriate to waive the numerical limitations on motions to reopen. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000). Furthermore, we find that the respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st. Cir. 1988), and has shown prejudice. See Rodrigues-Lariz, supra; Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). Therefore, in light of the evidence that the Notice to Appear was mailed to a slightly incorrect address and the holdings of the Ninth Circuit listed in this decision, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of relief under section 212(c) of the Act and any other form of relief form removal for which she is currently eligible.
 
*2 Accordingly, the unopposed motion is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.

2004 WL 1059583 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GENERO CARLOS CISNEROS-SALINAS ROSALINDA OLVERA-MARTINEZ
Files: A75 765 404 - Las Vegas
A75 765 405
March 16, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Jasmine C. Coca, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 19, 2002. On appeal, the respondents allege they have received ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
However, the respondents have failed to fulfil the prerequisites for allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondents have failed to attach to the appeal a sworn affidavit, signed by the respondents, setting out the alleged facts necessary to show a prima facie claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
This affidavit should have included the factual allegations underlying the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.1 The sworn affidavit should have also included the contractual agreement with the former counsel. The sworn affidavit should have also indicated that the respondents have notified the former counsel of the allegation of ineffective assistance, enabling the former counsel to respond to the allegations. In addition, the sworn affidavit should have indicated that a complaint has been filed against the former counsel with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Matter of Grijalva, supra; Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
We also note that the respondents have failed to identify how counsel’s action or inaction negatively affected the outcome of the respondents’ case. The respondents have failed to show that they would not be removable or would be eligible for relief but for the attorney’s action or inaction. See Matter of Assaad, supra. Consequently, the respondent has failed to show a prima facie claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the aliens fail to so depart, the aliens shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondents fail to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondents shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOORAJ MAAREF A.K.A. TOORAJ MARREF A.K.A. TOORAS MAAREF A.K.A. TORAJ MAREFF
File: A18 243 712 - San Francisco
March 15, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lena Ayoub, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia Spaletta
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on October 30, 2003, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s application for relief under the Convention Against Torture and finding the respondent ineligible for other relief from deportation for having been convicted of an aggravated felony considered to be a particularly serious crime. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen which will be denied.
 
Pursuant to regulation, a motion to reopen “shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.…” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (2003). In his motion to reopen, the respondent asserts that he has new and material evidence regarding his mental illness (bipolar disorder) which will result in torture if he is returned to Iran, as well as additional evidence supporting his previously made arguments that he will be tortured because he has renounced the Muslim faith, his length of stay in the United States, and his criminal history. The respondent argues that while all of this evidence was previously available, his former counsel failed to present it and, therefore, his case should be reopened due to ineffective assistance of counsel. In addition, the respondent asserts that he has met the procedural requirements for reopening under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).1 See also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
Although the respondent has met the general requirements for reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel, we do not find that he has established that he was prejudiced by his previous counsel. It is clear from the record that the respondent’s counsel asked to withdraw his representation and that the Department of Homeland Security opposed that motion (Tr. at 4-6). The Immigration Judge, however, granted the motion to withdraw because the respondent himself requested that his attorney be dismissed and that he be allowed to represent himself, despite knowing that the Immigration Judge intended to go forward with the hearing (Tr. at 9). Therefore, he can not now claim that his own failure to raise the issue of mental health as a possible basis to find that he will be tortured constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Moreover, the respondent’s former attorney has responded to the respondent’s bar complaint and indicated that he felt the documentary evidence submitted was sufficient, that the respondent’s mental health claim was novel but not necessarily one which would prevail, and that he felt that the key with regard to the respondent’s other claims lay in testimony. The fact that the respondent is now dissatisfied with his former counsel’s strategy in pursuing relief, when the respondent ultimately represented himself, is not grounds for reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

A motion to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, supra.

2004 WL 1059605 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RACHAEL EFURU ONUMONU
File: AXX XX5 742 - Philadelphia
March 15, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has requested reopening of proceedings before the Board of Immigration Appeals. We have jurisdiction over this matter pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The motion is denied.
 
We note that section 1003.2(c)(2) of 8 Code of Federal Regulations provides that
except as provided in paragraph (c)(3) of this section, a party may file only one motion to reopen proceedings (whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge) and that motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened, or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later.
 
 
The record reflects that the instant motion to reopen was filed with the Board on July 31, 2003. Because the respondent’s motion was filed more than 90 days after the date in which the final administrative decision by the Board was rendered, it is untimely and is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). We have also determined that the exceptions to these filing requirements set forth in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3) are inapplicable.
 
In her motion, the respondent also claims ineffective assistance of counsel. In the immigration area, there is a three-step procedure for filing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent is required to: (1) set forth the relevant facts in an affidavit; (2) demonstrate that she has informed counsel of her allegations and provided such counsel with an opportunity to respond; and (3) show that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, in cases where counsel allegedly violated his ethical or legal responsibilities. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). In addition to complying with the procedural requirements, a respondent must also show that, “he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance.” Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638.
 
We find that the respondent here has not complied with the mandates of Lozada and its progeny. The record reflects that she has not complied with the second and third procedural requirements as set out above. In addition, the respondent has failed to show that she was prejudiced by her representative’s performance. As the respondent has failed to satisfy all of the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set out in Matter of Lozada, supra, she has not made out a case for reopening. We further find that as the “new evidence” which the respondent seeks to have considered would not change the result, reopening is not warranted. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 1059609 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUS ELVA CABRAL HUISAR
File: AXX XX2 240 - Los Angeles
March 12, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alma Rosa Nieto, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal under 240A(b)
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 19, 2002. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had failed to submit her application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act within the time prescribed by the Immigration Judge and granted the respondent voluntary departure. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in pretermitting her application for cancellation of removal and requests a remand to the Immigration Judge to properly consider her application for cancellation of removal. The respondent also asserts that her former attorney provided ineffective assistance in failing to file her application for cancellation of removal in a timely manner. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
During the respondent’s May 11, 2001, hearing, the Immigration Judge instructed the respondent that her application for cancellation of removal must be filed by September 30, 2002, in order to be considered by the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 3). However, the respondent failed to submit her application prior to the deadline established by the Immigration Judge. As a result of the respondent’s failure to timely file her application for cancellation of removal, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent waived her right to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act. See I.J. at 2.
 
All documents and applications that are to be considered in a proceeding before an Immigration Judge must be filed with the Immigration Court having administrative control over the record of proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(a). The Immigration Judge has the authority to set deadlines for the submission of applications. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). If an application is not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application shall be deemed waived. See id; Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547 (BIA 1992). We find that the Immigration Judge properly pretermitted the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal, as the respondent failed to comply with the deadline set by the Immigration Judge to submit her application to the Immigration Court.
 
The respondent maintains that her failure to timely file her application for cancellation of removal is a result of the ineffective assistance of her former counsel. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Matter of Lozada, supra, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. However, the Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence); see also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 In addition to the three procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, it is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair: See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
In this matter, while the respondent has submitted an affidavit and provided evidence that her former attorney has been suspended by the California State Bar, the respondent has failed to establish that she was prejudiced as a result of her former attorney’s ineffectiveness. The respondent seeks cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act. However, we find that the respondent has failed to establish prima facie eligibility for cancellation of removal. The respondent has failed to submit a completed application for cancellation of removal or the necessary documentation to establish the requisite continuous physical presence, good moral character, or exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to a qualifying relative. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 1398798 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMIREZ ZALDIVAR, SAUL
File: AXX-XX2-453 - LOS ANGELES
March 12, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Friedman, John D., Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffery Finegan,
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated November 10, 2003. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998). See also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence). See also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
In the instant case, the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements above. He has also not shown that prior counsel neglected to present any evidence or argument to the Immigration Judge or the Board which may have changed the earlier decisions rendered in this case. Furthermore, the fact that prior counsel may have neglected to inform the respondent of his right to seek review of the Board’s earlier decision with the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals is not a reason for the Board to reopen. The respondent needs to raise the issue of the jurisdiction of the Ninth Circuit with the Ninth Circuit and not the Board. Finally, the respondent’s motion is barred by section 240B(d) of the Act even with the 30-day extension received from the DHS.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEANDRO PALOMAR
File: A76 249 376 - Chicago
March 11, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rosalba Pina, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(5)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(5)(A)(i)º - No valid labor certification
Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This record was last before us on August 8, 2002, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s May 25, 2000, decision to deny the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen his proceedings.
 
The respondent asserts in his motion that his proceedings should be reopened because he was denied ineffective assistance of counsel during his proceedings before the Immigration Court and on appeal. Our review of allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are governed by our analysis in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Matter of Lozada, we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement between the respondent and his legal representative and what actions were to be taken on his behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not.
 
We find that the respondent has complied with the above-described requirements. In addition, we acknowledge that his prior counsel, Mr. Gary Matthew Spraker, has been suspended from the practice of law before the Department of Homeland Security, the Immigration Courts, and this Board. We further find that the respondent has adequately established that he was not represented effectively by his prior counsel. Thus, we find that the respondent’s proceedings should be reopened so that he may have a further opportunity to pursue his application for relief from removal before the Immigration Court.1
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.

2004 WL 1398789 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YURIY VLADIMIROVICH BULANKOV
File: AXX XX7 679 - Los Angeles
March 11, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Irena Karpinski, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles Redman
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent initially filed a motion to reopen before the Immigration Court on January 11, 2001, which was denied on February 20, 2001. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 20, 2003, decision denying his second motion to reopen because it is number barred pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge should never have accepted and adjudicated his initial motion to reopen because it was filed with procedural deficiencies. In the alternative, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by failing to equitably toll the number limitation for motions. We find the respondent’s first argument to be without merit. The respondent, who is responsible for the procedural deficiencies in the first motion, cannot then exploit the deficiencies in an attempt to exempt himself from the time and number limitations. Even if we were to assume, arguendo, that the respondent’s first motion was not properly filed and treat it as if it never came before the Immigration Court, we would nonetheless deny the respondent’s current motion because it was filed out of time. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Thus, the respondent’s most recent motion would be barred by either the time limitation or the number limitation. The respondent stresses that the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has equitably tolled the time and number limitations for motions where an alien’s late petition was the result of deceptive actions by a nonattorney purporting to provide legal representation. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237, 1240 (9th Cir. 2000). More recently, the Ninth Circuit Court clarified that an alien seeking equitable tolling must include evidence of the fraud. See Carrillo-Gonzalez v. INS, 353 F.3d 1077, 1079 (9th Cir. 2003) (commenting that a fraud claim solely forwarded through the arguments of an alien’s counsel does not constitute evidence). Unlike the aliens in Lopez or Varela, the respondent has not provided evidence of the agreement between him and his former “counsel,” nor has he attempted to comply with the additional requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Lopez v. INS, supra, at 1099; Varela v. INS, supra, at 1239. Consequently, we conclude that the time and number limitations apply to the respondent’s case and agree with the Immigration Judge’s ultimate conclusion that the respondent’s most recent motion is statutorily barred.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 880276 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DANIEL MANUEL TAPIA-AGIPE A.K.A. DANIEL MANUEL TAPIA
File: AXX XX1 099 - Florida
March 10, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kenneth Panzer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anthony E. Maingot
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a motion to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The record is remanded.
 
The record reflects that on August 25, 2003, the respondent appeared before an Immigration Judge, conceded removability, and requested an opportunity for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The respondent was then instructed to file his application (Form I-191) with the Immigration Court no later than September 8, 2003. The application was not filed by the deadline, and on September 26, 2003, the Immigration Judge determined that the request for section 212(c) relief was abandoned. Specifically, the Immigration Judge noted that “the respondent failed to timely submit his application for relief and failed to request an extension” (I.J. at 1-2). The Immigration Judge further noted that the respondent requested that the court accept his untimely application after the deadline for submission had already passed (I.J. at 2). Finally, the Immigration Judge observed that the court “admonished the respondent and his ¿ formerº attorney of the consequences of not timely filing his application” (I.J. at 2).
 
On October 29, 2003, the respondent appealed the Immigration Judge’s above-referenced decision. On January 4, 2004, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to remand asserting ineffective assistance of counsel. Subsequently, the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration of Naturalization Service) filed an opposition to the respondent’s motion. On February 9, 2004, the respondent filed a response to the DHS’ opposition.
 
We will first address whether the respondent’s application for a section 212(c) waiver of inadmissibility was properly found to have been abandoned on September 8, 2003. We note that documents and applications that are to be considered in a proceeding before an Immigration Judge must be filed with the Immigration Court having administrative control over the record of proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(a). In addition, the Immigration Judge may set and extend time limits for the filing of applications and related documents and responses thereto, if any. If an application is not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application shall be deemed waived. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c); Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547 (BIA 1992). Based on the foregoing, we find that the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent abandoned his application for a section 212(c) waiver of inadmissibility by not filing it in a timely manner. Matter of R-R-, supra.
 
*2 We will now address whether the failure of the respondent to timely file his section 212(c) application was on account of attorney negligence. We note that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640; see also Matter of Aassad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561 (BIA 2003).
 
We have reviewed the record and note that the respondent has satisfied the requirements set forth above. We further note that the record reflects the following. The respondent was a lawful permanent resident of the United States since 1988, and had no other relief from removal available. The respondent’s former attorney was given 2 weeks to file the section 212(c) application with the Immigration Court, but neglected to do so. Nowhere in the respondent’s former attorney’s correspondence is there any suitable explanation for why the application was not timely filed. As noted by the respondent, his former attorney vaguely explained that exceptional circumstances prevented him from timely filing the respondent’s 212(c) application (Respondent’s Affidavit at 4; Respondent’s Motion for Leave of Court at 1). We further note that the respondent’s former attorney indicated that his untimely filing of the respondent’s 212(c) application was “through no fault of the respondent” (Respondent’s Motion for Leave of Court at 1; Respondent’s Affidavit at 4). As a result of the respondent’s former attorney’s negligence, the respondent’s only opportunity to obtain a waiver of his inadmissibility was lost.
 
We find that the respondent has established that his failure to file his section 212(c) application by the date required was as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, supra; Matter of Aassad, supra. Accordingly, the record is remanded to allow the respondent an opportunity for section 212(c) consideration.

IN RE: ALEJANDRO TECUAPETLA-PANI

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS

ON BEHALF OF DHS:

*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, moves the Board to reopen proceedings so that he may apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”). The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia on October 15, 1998, and denied the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen on August 25, 2000. We affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen on June 19, 2001. The respondent filed the instant motion to reopen with the Board on September 22, 2003, in which he argues that his in absentia order of removal should be rescinded and the untimely motion should be granted because of exceptional circumstances and equitable tolling due to ineffective assistance of counsel. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 3-5. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the respondent’s motion to reopen. See DHS Opposition to Motion to Reopen. The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent failed to file his current motion to reopen within 180 days of the Board’s June 19, 2001, decision as required by section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3) and 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Furthermore, we also find that the 180-day statute of limitation in the respondent’s case is not equitably tolled, under Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999), because he failed to demonstrate that this motion was untimely as a result of deceptive actions by a notary posing as an attorney. See Lopez v. INS, supra. Additionally, we note that although the respondent claims that he failed to appear before the Immigration Judge because of a notary’s ineffective assistance of counsel, he failed to meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) and failed to include an application for cancellation of removal or any other relief with any of his motions to reopen. See Lopez v. INS, supra, at 1099-1101.
 
Accordingly, in light of the foregoing discussion, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 880265 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KIRA KOUZNETSOVA
File: AXX XX2 597 - New York
March 9, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Natalie S. Tynan, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on December 24, 2003. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on June 20, 2002. The motion has been filed out of time and will be denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion.
 
The respondent claims in her motion that prior counsel was incompetent, because prior counsel failed to file a brief with this Board, which caused us to dismiss her appeal without opinion. There is, however, no basis for abrogating the motions deadline here. Any equitable tolling of the motions deadline is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence on her own behalf. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 127, 134-35. (2nd Cir. 2000). The pending motion was filed over a year late. The respondent has been represented by current counsel by no later than April, 2003 - when counsel filed a motion to withdraw a previous motion to reopen filed by the respondent. Current counsel received a copy of the respondent’s file in July, 2003 (Respondent’s Mot., at p. 5). Nevertheless, the respondent did not submit a complaint against former counsel with New York authorities until September, 2003, and did not file the pending motion until December 24, 2003. Id.
 
For claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Esposito v. INS, 987 F.2d 108, 110 (2d Cir. 1993). The respondent claims that we should excuse the lateness of the motion, because she was awaiting a response from former counsel to her complaints (Respondent’s Mot., at p. 7). Yet Matter of Lozada does not require that a respondent delay filing a motion for that reason. Moreover, as noted, the respondent delayed filing her complaint against former counsel until months after engaging current counsel. Moreover, she waited over a month after filing her “rebuttal response” with the New York authorities to file the pending motion. Id. There is no basis for abrogating the motions deadline here. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.
