IN RE: AZ SHAMIM HYDER NESSA MEHERUN SHAMIM HYDER TAHSIN SHAMIM HYDER
Files: A70 637 541 - Los Angeles
A72 516 716
A72 516 718
March 8, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Shawn Sedaghat, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Monica M. Little
Assistant District Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 This matter was last before this Board on July 31, 1998, when we denied as untimely the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The respondent1 now files a motion to reopen and remand asserting ineffective assistance of counsel and changed country conditions. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) filed an opposition to the respondent’s motion. The respondent’s motion will be granted.
 
The respondent is a male native and citizen of Pakistan. In his motion to reopen, the respondent alleges that his former attorney prejudiced him, thus warranting the reopening of his case. Amongst other things, the respondent charges that his former attorney sent an ill prepared attorney who the respondent never met before to represent the respondent at his merits hearing. Additionally, the respondent asserts that his former attorney abandoned him and failed to timely file the respondent’s appeal. The respondent submitted an article indicating that the California Bar Association suspended the respondent’s former attorney from the practice of law for, inter alia, “engag ¿ ingº in a course of practicing law that was reckless and involved gross negligence” (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen and Remand; Tab B). The respondent argues that he was further prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of a paralegal.
 
While we offer no opinion as to the merits of the respondent’s applications for relief, we recognize that the respondent’s appeal proffers a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (noting several required steps which should be taken to successfully prove a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel). See Respondent’s Motion and Respondent’s Motion to Accept Supplemental Filing. Furthermore, regardless of the specific Lozada requirements, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this matter arises, has held that where the record shows a “clear and obvious” case of ineffective assistance of counsel, the requirements of Lozada need not be “rigidly enforced.” See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F. 3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (stating that “Lozada is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one”). See also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir. 2000) (noting that the Lozada requirements are reasonable but “not dispositive,” depending on the circumstances of each case).
 
*2 The record supports the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Therefore, we find it appropriate to reopen these proceedings and remand this matter to afford the respondent a full and fair hearing on the merits.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

2004 WL 880233 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ESEQUIEL MAGANA-MOJICA
File: AXX XX6 870 - Seattle
March 8, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marie Higuera, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tammy L. Fitting
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
The respondent timely appeals an Immigration Judge’s April 26, 2002, decision denying cancellation of removal. Removability is not at issue. The appeal will be sustained, the motion granted, and the proceeding remanded.
 
During the proceeding below, the respondent admitted that he pushed his wife and was convicted for domestic violence (Tr. at 10, 34-35, 39). In addition, the Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) submitted Form I-213, which stated that the respondent was convicted of domestic violence in December 1999 (Exh. 3). Based on this evidence, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent had been convicted of the crime of domestic violence, and that the respondent was therefore statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(b)(b)(1)(C) (I. J. at 2). The Immigration Judge further found that, but for the respondent’s conviction, he would be eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, and granted the respondent voluntary departure (I.J. at 2-5).
 
During the pendency of this appeal, the respondent submitted new evidence—a motion for relief from judgment because of ineffective assistance of counsel, which was filed with the Washington State Court, and an uncertified copy of his modified conviction record. According to the modified conviction record, the respondent was convicted of “assault.” We construe the respondent’s submission of additional evidence as a motion to reopen and remand. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2; see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Since this evidence is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing, we will grant the motion to reopen.
 
Section 240A(b)(1)(C) of the Act provides, as relevant here, that an alien is ineligible for cancellation of removal if convicted of an offense under section 237(a)(2) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2), which includes the crime of domestic violence. Section 237(a)(2)(E) of the Act describes the crime of domestic violence, as relevant here, as any crime of violence (as defined in section 16 of title 18, United States Code) against a person committed by a current spouse of the person. Section 16 of title 18 (U.S.C.) defines a crime of violence as (a) an offense that has as an element the use, attempted use, or threatened use of physical force against the person or property of another, or (b) any other force that is a felony and that, by its nature, involves a substantial risk that physical force against the person or property of another may be used in the course of committing the offense.
 
*2 Under the Ninth Circuit law, where an appeal in this case would rest, the modified conviction record is the conviction for purposes of the respondent’s immigration proceeding. See Garcia-Lopez v. Ashcroft, 334 F.3d 840 (9th Cir. 2003). The modified conviction does not indicate, however, whether the respondent was convicted of assault in the first, second, third, or fourth degree. See Wash. Rev. Code Ann. §§ 9A.36.041; 9A.36.031; 9A.36.021; 9A.36.011. On the record as it now stands, it is unclear whether the respondent’s conviction was a crime of violence as defined in 18 U.S.C. § 16 because there are varying degrees of assault in Washington State, some assault convictions may not fall under the definition of crime of violence, and it is unclear under which assault statute the respondent was convicted.
 
Accordingly, we will sustain the appeal, and remand this proceeding for a determination of whether the respondent’s modified conviction constitutes a crime of violence. In reaching his decision, we recommend that the Immigration Judge take into account whether the respondent, on remand, submits a certified copy of the modified conviction.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The proceeding is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Videz-Ruiz v. Gonzales, 9th Cir., December 14, 2005
2004 WL 880371 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO VIDEZ-RUIZ
File: AXX XX3 693 - San Francisco
March 5, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert G. Berke, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kenneth S. Sogabe
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on August 26, 2003, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal as untimely and returned the record to the Immigration Court without further action. The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reopen proceedings with this Board on October 6, 2003. The Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent’s attorney, who is the same attorney who failed to file a timely appeal, argues that a member of his staff mailed the Notice of Appeal to the incorrect address and, thus, the motion to reopen should be granted due to ineffective assistance of counsel. Claims of ineffective assistance of counsel require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. See Matter of Assad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), cf. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10(1st. Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Id. The respondent has failed to submit an affidavit and has offered no reason why he could not have filed an appropriate affidavit. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 2004 WL 253539 (9th Cir. 2004) (ineffective assistance of counsel claim denied where alien: failed to file affidavit; failed to offer any reason why he could not have filed an appropriate affidavit; and, he failed to provide notice of the claim to counsel); see also Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 938 (9th Cir. 2003)(court found ineffective assistance of counsel where both aliens and counsel filed affidavits). While respondent’s counsel appears to be on notice of the ineffective assistance of counsel claim since he conceded in the motion that the appeal was not timely filed, counsel has not submitted an affidavit attesting to the mistake. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 825 (9 th Cir. 2003)(court found alien complied with second prong of Lozada where counsel conceded his mistake in the motion and his accompanying declaration). Furthermore, the respondent has not provided any evidence that he has filed a State Bar complaint against counsel or provided any explanation as to why he has not filed a complaint. Accordingly, the respondent has failed to substantially comply with the requirements set forth in Lozada. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, supra at 824 (court has permitted ineffective assistance of counsel claims to go forward when there is substantial compliance with Lozada).
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record of proceeding is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.

2004 WL 880357 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SASHI LATA SHARMA
File: A70 544 970 - San Francisco
March 5, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ashwani K. Bhakhri, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Grace H. Cheung
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board when in a decision dated January 8, 2004, we denied the respondent’s1 motion to reopen as numerically barred and untimely filed and rejected the respondent’s argument that we should accept her motion sua sponte, noting that the respondent had not shown exceptional circumstances sufficient to cause the Board to exercise the limited discretionary authority to overlook the time and number bar and reopen. See Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373, 380 n.9 (A.G. 2002) (citing Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997)). This case is now before the Board again based on a motion to reconsider our denial of her last motion to reopen. The motion is denied.
 
When deciding a motion to reconsider, the Board considers the case as though the decision in the case on the record before us had never been entered. If the respondent was eligible for relief at the time of the original decision, then in reconsidering the decision, we treat her status as that which it had been at the time of the initial decision. “The very nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard.” Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991). We have reviewed the record, as well as both the Immigration Judge’s and this Board’s prior decisions. We are not persuaded by the respondent’s contentions that our prior decision was either legally, or factually, erroneous. The respondent has failed to demonstrate that reconsideration is warranted on that basis.
 
Moreover, to the extent the respondent alleges ineffective assistance by former counsel, we are unpersuaded. We note that in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), we laid out the requirements that a respondent must meet in order to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997). Here, the respondent has failed to comply with any of the established requirements to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Moreover, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that she suffered prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. We recognize that the present case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which has held that rigid adherence to the procedural requirements established in Lozada is not always necessary. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir 2000). However, where the respondent has failed to comply with any of the Lozada requirements and where the respondent has also failed to show any prejudice, we are compelled to deny the motion to reopen. See Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
*2 Moreover, even if the respondent had persuaded us that she was unaware of the filing of the March 6, 1998, motion to reopen, she has not indicated how she pursued her rights diligently, or how she was prevented from filing her own motion to reopen after she received the November 23, 1998, “bag and baggage” letter, so as to allow for any consideration of “equitable tolling” of the statute. See Ituribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (in order for equitable tolling to apply, the invoking party must show that she was prevented from properly filing a motion to reopen because of fraud, deception, or error); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001) (in order for equitable tolling to apply, respondent needs to show that her ignorance was caused by circumstances beyond her control, and which go beyond a claim of excusable neglect.). The arguments contained in the respondent’s motion were previously considered, and rejected.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 880366 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA DEL CARMEN MONJE SERNA
File: AXX XX0 473 - Los Angeles
March 5, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jason R. Grimm
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reconsider has been filed out of time and will be denied. The Board’s last order in these proceedings was entered on December 10, 2003. A motion to reconsider a decision of the Board must be filed within 30 days after the date of that decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). In the instant case, a motion to reconsider would have been due on or before January 9, 2004. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until January 15, 2004. The motion to reconsider was therefore filed out of time.
 
Respondent asserts that the Board’s order essentially summarily affirms the Immigration Judge’s opinion, and violates her due process rights because the Board has not explained its rationale for its ruling. A motion of this nature, however, is statutorily barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 3.2(b)(3). A summary affirmance without opinion concludes that any error in the immigration judge’s decision was harmless or immaterial; the immigration judge’s order provides the rationale, and thus the legitimacy, for the Board’s summary affirmance.
 
In her motion, the respondent has raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, the motion must establish prejudice arising from the alleged failings of prior counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent here has failed to meet these requirements.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.

2004 WL 880324 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VIDAL AMILCAR RAMIREZ-SALMERON A.K.A. VIDAL A. SALMERON A.K.A. VIDAL A. RAMIREZ A.K.A. VIDAL H. RAMIREZ A.K.A. JOSE RAMIREZ A.K.A. AMILCAR VIDAL A.K.A SALMERON RAMIREZ
File: AXX XX7 177 - Newark
March 4, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David E. Piver, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent moves this Board to reopen our February 12, 2003, decision in which we summarily dismissed his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s October 2, 2002, decision. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”) (I.J. at 3-4). Furthermore, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act and adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act because the respondent did not possess an approved visa petition (I.J. at 3-4). In his motion to reopen, the respondent argues that his previous attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel because the attorney failed to notify him that his visa petition interview was scheduled for April 16, 2002, and failed to notify him of the Board’s February 12, 2003 decision. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 11-21. The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
We have held that in order to reopen proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel, a respondent must demonstrate that he or she was prejudiced by prior counsel’s conduct. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 640 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In the instant case, the respondent has never possessed an approved visa petition and, therefore, remains ineligible for a section 212(h) waiver and adjustment of status. See Matter of Michel, 21 I&N Dec. 1101, 1103 (BIA 1998) (discussing the need for an approved visa petition when applying for a section 212(h) waiver and adjustment of status). Moreover, the respondent has failed to demonstrate in his motion to reopen that he is currently eligible for any relief from removal or that there was an error in the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Matter of Assaad, supra, at 562. We also note that the respondent had an opportunity on appeal to claim ineffective assistance of counsel relating to his visa petition and argue eligibility for a section 212(h) waiver, but failed to do so. See Respondent’s Notice of Appeal. For the aforementioned reasons, we find that the respondent failed to demonstrate prejudice stemming from his prior counsel’s conduct.
 
*2 Accordingly, in light of the foregoing discussion, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 880329 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MERVYN ADOLPHUS APPLEWHITE
File: A31 171 726 - San Diego
March 4, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brian Figeroux, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alan S. Rabinowitz
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on March 26, 2001, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that his application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) had been abandoned and ordering him deported to Trinidad. On February 12, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and reconsider and a motion to stay his removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a response in opposition to the motion. The motion to reopen and reconsider is denied. The motion for a stay of removal is denied.
 
In his motions, the respondent seeks reopening of proceedings to apply for three forms of relief which he contends became available after the Immigration Judge ordered him deported on June 25, 1996: a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act, cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), and derivative United States citizenship.1 Specifically, he argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel because former counsel failed to submit an application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act by the deadline set by the Immigration Judge. The respondent contends that under Ninth Circuit case law, he does not have to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel where the record clearly demonstrates that an attorney failed to meet the standard of competent representation. He additionally asserts that he can file a motion to reopen based upon new law or intervening circumstances that might change the result in a case, as in his case where there have been significant changes regarding section 212(c) waivers, including the statutory elimination of such relief for certain criminal aliens and the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, 533, U.S. 289 (2001) (holding that section 212(c) waivers remain available to aliens who pled guilty prior to April 24, 1996) since the Board dismissed his appeal. He further claims to be eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, which form of relief did not come into existence until April 1, 1997.
 
*2 The respondent’s motion is untimely. Motions to reconsider must be filed within 30 days of the Board’s March 26, 2001, decision while motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days of that decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The respondent’s motions were filed on February 12, 2004, a date well after the regulatory required deadlines. The respondent argues, however, that the intervening changes in law allow him to file motions outside of these deadlines. However, the most recent of the alleged intervening changes relied upon by the respondent took place on June 12, 2001, a date less than 90 days after our decision, and therefore within the time period for the filing of a motion to reopen, and over 2 1/2 years before the filing of the present motions.2 We are aware that the United States Court of appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in which jurisdiction the present case arises, has held that the time restrictions for the filing of motions before the Board are subject to equitable tolling where aliens act diligently. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). Equitable tolling applies where aliens have acted diligently. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d at 897. We have addressed the issue of significant changes in law affecting relief for aliens. See Matter of X-G-W-, 22 I&N Dec. 71 (BIA 1998). However, as we observed in Matter of G-C-L-, the purpose of removing the time and number restrictions on motions filed due to intervening changes in law was to “afford individuals both a means to promptly respond to the change in law and a reasonable period of time in which to do so.” See Matter of G-C-L-, 23 I&N Dec. 359, 362 (BIA 2002). The respondent has not acted diligently in filing a motion to reopen on February 12, 2004, based upon intervening changes in law, the most recent of which occurred on June 12, 2001. We therefore find his motions to be untimely.
 
The respondent also contends, however, that his motions should be equitably tolled because he received ineffective assistance from his former counsel. He contends that he would have applied for a section 212(c) waiver by the deadline set by the Immigration Judge but for the ineffectiveness of his former counsel. The Ninth Circuit has held that the Lozada requirements should not be applied rigidly, particularly where the record establishes a clear and obvious ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 82 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002), and cases cited therein. The Ninth Circuit also has stated that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevent from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d at 897. See also Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, supra, and cases cited therein. We find that this record, however, does not shows clear and obvious ineffective assistance of counsel which resulted in prejudice to the respondent. The Board’s March 26, 2001, decision clearly indicated that we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the respondent had abandoned his section 212(c) application because he had not filed it by the deadline set by the Immigration Judge. Thus, the respondent knew upon receipt of our decision that his former attorney had not filed his application. The ineffectiveness of counsel was therefore apparent to the respondent at that time. Moreover, the Supreme Court issued its decision in INS v. St. Cyr, supra, only 2 1/2 months after we issued our decision. The respondent therefore could have filed a motion to reopen based upon former counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness within a reasonable period of time from the Supreme Court’s decision, if not within 90 days of either that decision or our decision. The respondent has not acted diligently by filing his motions over 2 1/2 years after the Supreme Court’s decision. For all of the above reasons, the respondent’s motions are denied as untimely.
 
*3 The respondent also argues that he has derived United States citizenship from his naturalized parents under section 320 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1431, as amended by the Child Citizenship Act of 2000, Pub. L. No. 106-395, 114 Stat. 1631 (enacted Oct. 30, 2000, effective Feb. 27, 2001). We find that the respondent has not met his burden of establishing that he derived United States citizenship from his parents. First, he has not shown how any of the facts involved regarding his purported United States citizenship are new or previously unavailable or undiscoverable. Second, he provides no documentation to support his claim, such as his birth certificate, his parents’ marriage certificate, his parents’ naturalization certificates, his parents’ divorce decree, and any legal documentation regarding their separation and his custody. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) (motions to reopen must be accompanied by new or previously unavailable or undiscoverable evidence to support the relief requested). Third, the provisions of the Child Citizenship Act are not retroactive. See Hughes v. Ashcroft, 255 F.3d 752, 760 (9th Cir. 2001). As the respondent was over the age of 18 years upon the effective date of the Child Citizenship Act (February 27, 2001), its provisions are inapplicable to him and he must satisfy the citizenship requirements as in existence prior that amendment. The respondent does not contend that he has derived United States citizenship based upon the provisions former section 321 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1432, which provision applies to him. Nor do we find that he has provided evidence showing that either both of his parents naturalized prior to his attaining 18 years of age or that his parents were legally separated and that his mother had legal custody of him. See Barthelemy v. Ashcroft, 329 F.3d 1062, 1065 (9th Cir. 2003) (legal separation means a judicial decree affecting the marital relationship). Therefore, his motion to reopen based upon a claim of derivative United States citizenship must be denied.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motions are denied.

He also says that he was convicted on November 20, 2001, of resisting arrest, for which he was sentenced to 3 years imprisonment as a persistent felony offender. Id. 2004 WL 880338 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR SANDOVAL ALMA ALICIA SANDOVAL
File: AXX XX0 435 - Las Vegas
AXX XX0 436
March 4, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Sarah J. M. Jones, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Elizabeth T. Rawlings
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondents have filed a motion to reopen. The motion will be denied. The final order in these proceedings, in which we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision, was entered by the Board on September 16, 2003.
 
The respondents claim in their motion to reopen that their prior counsel did not properly prepare an appellate brief before the Board due to shortcomings in the transcript.
 
Upon our review of the record, it does not appear that the respondents’ former counsel rendered ineffective assistance merely because they did not receive the desired outcome from their appeal. “Ineffective assistance of counsel in a deportation proceeding is a denial of due process under the Fifth Amendment if the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.” Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir.1999) (quoting Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985)). To succeed, due process challenges to deportation proceedings require a showing of prejudice. See Ortiz, 179 F.3d at 1153. “Prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Id. (citing Barraza-Rivera v. INS, 913 F.2d 1443, 1448 (9th Cir.1990)). The Immigration Judge denied the respondents’ applications for cancellation of removal because they had failed to establish the “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship,” element. In representing the respondents, prior counsel submitted a brief before us contending that the respondents had met this element. We concur with the Department of Homeland Security’s position in its brief in opposition, that the respondents have failed to establish any instance of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondents have manifestly failed to establish that former counsel’s representation was “so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Id.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondents the relief of cancellation of removal because he found that the respondents failed to establish exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to their three United States citizen children if they are removed from the United States. Upon further review of the transcript, we find, as we did previously, that the testimony was sufficiently clear to judge the merits of the respondents’ appeal. Moreover, the respondents have not established that the transcript prejudiced their appeal. See Ortiz v. INS, supra. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
  
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 880345 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BIRRUETE-PENA, JUAN
File: AXX-XX3-522 - CHICAGO
March 4, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
VILLASUSO JR., RAUL
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated December 16, 2003. The respondent, by and through new counsel, has filed a motion to reopen to present previously unavailable evidence which supports his claim for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. A motion to reopen proceedings shall not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent has failed to demonstrate that the proffered evidence was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. For example, the motion is supported by the respondent’s daughter’s medical records dated 1996 through1998, long before the respondent’s January 6, 2003, removal hearing. The respondent admits that the evidence was previously available, but he claims that he only recently realized its significance. The respondent’s claim does not excuse his failure to present the evidence at his removal hearing. Furthermore, he was represented by counsel at the hearing and on appeal. He has not raised an ineffective assistance of counsel claim either explicitly or implicitly. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Inasmuch as the respondent’s motion is supported by new evidence, he has failed to establish that the such evidence is material. The respondent states that he had surgery on July 4, 2003. He submits two medical records dated July 4, 2003. See Respondent’s Motion at 2, Exhibits 2 through 5. It is impossible to discern from these documents the nature and extent of the respondent’s July 4, 2003, procedure. In any event, for purposes of section 240A(b) relief, the respondent must show exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to his qualifying family members, not himself. In short, the respondent’s motion does not meet the regulatory requirements for reopening. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 880307 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHAHANA AKHTER
File: A73 602 504 - New York
March 3, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Freddy Jacobs, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leslie S. Evans
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Nonimmigrant - remained longer than permitted
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on November 18, 2003, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s March 10, 2003, decision denying a motion to reopen these proceedings. In our order, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision. We also concluded that the respondent had not established that her failure to appear was due to ineffective assistance of counsel and that her motion for newly-acquired relief was untimely.
 
The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider our November 18, 2003, order. Pursuant to the federal regulations, a motion to reconsider should specify the claimed errors of fact or law in the Board’s prior order and should be supported by pertinent authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) (2003); Zhao v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 265 F.3d 83, 90 (2001); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991), aff’d, 979 F.2d 212 (11th Cir. 1992) (table case). The respondent argues in her motion, as she did in her appeal, that the motion should be granted because she is now eligible for adjustment of status. We have already considered and decided this issue. Further, the respondent has not identified errors of fact or law in our order.
 
In addition to reconsideration, the respondent seeks reopening. In this regard, the respondent included a document for consideration, indicating that she has a second United States citizen child. However, notwithstanding this new evidence, the motion exceeds the numerical limitations for motions to reopen. On March 10, 2003, the Immigration Judge denied a motion to reopen filed by the respondent. The regulations provide that motions to reopen shall be limited to one motion to reopen, with certain exceptions not pertinent here. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). To the extent that this is the respondent’s second motion to reopen, it is barred by the regulations.1
 
Moreover, although the Board retains limited discretionary powers under the regulations to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte in unique situations where it would serve the interests of justice, we have considered the arguments set forth in the respondent’s motion in light of the record before us but have concluded that reopening is not justified. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) (2003); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s first motion to reopen was denied because it was untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Even if this motion to reopen was not barred by the numerical limitation, the respondent has not shown that it was timely.

2004 WL 848522 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIRANDA-PAREDES, SALVADOR
File: AXX-XX5-498 - SAN DIEGO
March 1, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rodriguez, Marco Antonio, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kathryn E. Stuever,
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 10, 2003. Following review of the arguments advanced on appeal, we are unpersuaded to disturb the Immigration Judge’s January 10, 2003, decision. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (regarding clear and obvious cases of ineffective assistance of counsel). Although the respondent raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he failed comply with all of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and we do not find this a case in which the requirements should be overlooked. The record reflects that the respondent was present when the Immigration Judge announced the additional documents that were needed to support his adjustment application that needed to be filed within 90 days (Tr. at 16-18). The documents were not filed in a timely manner. Regarding the late submission, respondent’s then counsel stated: “There really is no excuse. I got documents late, but it’s my fault that it’s that late (Tr. at 26).” The record also indicates that the attorney had not received various documents (including the respondent’s pay stub and high school diploma) until the day of the hearing (Tr. at 27). Thus, there are indications in the record that the respondent did not provide documents to counsel in a manner which would have allowed their timely filing. The burden of clarifying such matters is on the respondent, who is now represented by new counsel. Yet the present motion neither clearly sets forth the claimed circumstances resulting in the late filing of the application and supporting documents nor provides prior counsel the opportunity to respond to any claim that such delay was attributable to his inadequate representation. We are not persuaded that failure to comply with the not burdensome procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada should excused or ignored on the record before us. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the alien is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*2 NOTICE: If the alien fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the alien shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Borges v. Gonzales, 3rd Cir., March 30, 2005
2004 WL 848509 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE A. BORGES
File: AXX XX1 940 - New York
March 1, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul O’Dwyer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa M. Golub
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - Nonimmigrant - remained longer than permitted
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration and reopening
 
In a decision dated April 17, 2003, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The respondent has appealed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
A brief procedural chronology follows. On February 3, 1998, an Immigration Judge issued an in absentia order of removal against the respondent when he failed to appear for the scheduled hearing. On May 21, 1998, the respondent, through former counsel, moved to reconsider the decision. On June 10, 1998, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion finding that the respondent had failed to establish exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. On January 24, 2003, the respondent, through present counsel, filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of his former counsel. On March 10, 2003, the Immigration Judge denied the motion for two reasons. First, the motion was found to be untimely. Second, the Immigration Judge held that the respondent had failed to comply with the procedural requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). On April 2, 2003, the respondent moved to reconsider the Immigration Judge’s decision, and on April 17, 2003, the motion was denied because it was untimely filed, and because of the Lozada defect.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen due to non-compliance with Matter of Lozada. An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), maybe rescinded only upon a motion to reopen, filed within 180 days, which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The Immigration Judge correctly determined in denying the motion to reopen that the motion was untimely filed, and that in absentia proceedings were properly conducted.
 
On appeal, the respondent claims that an individual in his former counsel’s office told him that he did not have to appear for the removal hearing. The respondent thus claims that there were exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. However, the motion to reopen proceedings was untimely. The respondent’s motion was submitted by present counsel on January 24, 2003, far more than 180 days after the Immigration Judge’s February 3, 1998, in absentia decision.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent claims that his former counsel’s ineffectiveness constitutes an exception to the time and numerical bars on motions to reopen, the Board has held that an ineffective assistance claim does not qualify as an exception to the 180-day statutory limit for the filing of a motion to reopen or rescind an in absentia order of deportation. Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998); Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec 113 (BIA 1998). The respondent in his affidavit admits that he was informed of the scheduled hearing date, but he failed to attend due to incorrect statements by an individual in former counsel’s office. The respondent, however, had the obligation to attend the scheduled hearing. See also Bejar v. Ashcroft, 324 F.3d 127 (3d Cir. 2003) (no exceptional circumstances shown where alien’s former counsel was notified of the hearing). The respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely and the Immigration Judge properly denied the motion to reopen and the subsequent motion to reconsider.
 
We also agree that the respondent’s motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was not properly made since the respondent failed to comply with the procedural requirements set out in Matter of Lozada. The fact that the respondent subsequently complied with those procedural requirements cannot remedy this fundamental defect. Finally, we have considered the respondent’s affidavit and the allegations he made against his former counsel. We have also considered the affidavit of the respondent’s former counsel, which was filed in response to the respondent’s bar complaint wherein he vigorously disputes the respondent’s allegations against him. See Ponce-Levia v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 369, 377 (3d Cir. 2003) (to advance a successful claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate prejudice--he “must show that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.”). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In the case at hand former counsel’s affidavit has the ring of truth. Former counsel disputes the essential allegations made by the respondent. Former counsel writes that he never told his employee to tell the respondent that he should not appear in court for the scheduled hearing. Former counsel makes a convincing presentation at 4 of his affidavit that it would not have been logical for either himself or his employee who has since retired to negligently tell the respondent not to appear for the scheduled hearing. Former counsel also points out that the respondent received the notice of hearing from the Immigration Court which warned the respondent of the penalties for his failure to appear. In fact a copy of that hearing notice appears in the record of proceeding and is dated January 27, 1998. We find, therefore, that the respondent has not established the element of prejudice in his attempt to establish ineffective assistance of his former counsel. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted by Ok Hee Kim v. Gonzales, 9th Cir., July 11, 2006
2004 WL 848513 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OK HEE KIM
File: A75 466 422 - Honolulu
February 27, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lawrence Sizemore, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
June Y.I. Ito
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; remand; waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h); voluntary departure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of South Korea, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s September 10, 2002, denial of her applications for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h) and adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) should have been equitably estopped from placing her into removal proceedings; that she did not receive a full and fair hearing because her motion to change venue was denied and because she received ineffective assistance of counsel and an incompetent interpreter; that section 212(h) of the Act is unconstitutional; and that the Immigration Judge erred in not granting her voluntary departure.
 
The first issue we consider is whether the DHS should have been equitably estopped from placing the respondent into removal proceedings. This Board is without authority to apply the doctrine of equitable estoppel against the DHS so as to preclude it from undertaking a lawful course of action that it is empowered to pursue by statute and regulation. Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335 (BIA 1991). The respondent does not allege that the DHS acted beyond its authority. Moreover, it is well within the DHS’s authority and prosecutorial discretion to initiate removal proceedings. Matter of Ramirez-Somera, 20 I&N Dec. 564 (BIA 1992); see also Matter of U-M-, 20 I&N Dec. 327 (BIA 1991); Matter of Torres, 19 I&N Dec. 371 (BIA 1986); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980); Matter of Geronimo, 13 I&N Dec. 680 (BIA 1971).
 
The second issue we consider is whether the respondent received a full and fair hearing. The respondent claims that the denial of her motion to change venue, the ineffective assistance of her attorneys,1 and an incompetent interpreter deprived her of a full and fundamentally fair hearing in violation of her right to due process.
 
*2 With respect to the motion to change venue, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that motions to change venue are left to the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge, but an arbitrary refusal to change venue can be a violation of the statutory right to a reasonable opportunity to attend and present evidence at the deportation hearing. Campos v. Nail, 43 F.3d 1285 (9th Cir. 1994), citing Baires v. INS, 856 F.2d 89, 92 (9th Cir. 1988). In this case, the Immigration Judge did not make an arbitrary decision. The Immigration Judge considered the respondent’s circumstances that her residence and business were in Maryland as was her disabled husband who was supposed to testify on her behalf. Tr. at 11-24. However, on the day the Immigration Judge considered the motion, the respondent and her attorneys were already present in court and were scheduled to proceed with a merits hearing. Also, the Immigration Judge was willing to allow the respondent’s husband to testify via telephone. Thus, the respondent was not deprived of a full and fair hearing due to the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to change venue. We also find that the respondent was not deprived a full and fair hearing due to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, the Ninth Circuit has held that to show a deprivation of due process caused by ineffective assistance of counsel, the alien must show that counsel’s ineffective performance prejudiced her. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2002). Prejudice results when the “performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Id., citing Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999).
 
Although the respondent has complied with the Lozada requirements, she cannot show that the performance of her attorneys was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.2 Id. The respondent claims that instead of raising the substantive issue of whether her crimes arose out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct for purposes of removability, her attorneys conceded removability without advising her of the consequences and without her consent. The respondent also claims that she was not advised of the option to seek voluntary departure in lieu of a removal proceeding in order to avoid having to establish good moral character. Finally, the respondent claims that because her attorneys transferred her file to her new attorney in Maryland before the Immigration Judge decided the motion to change venue, they were completely unprepared for the merits hearing and were unable to submit any evidence or documents in support of her claim. Respondent’s Brief at 32-36.
 
*3 We first consider the issue of removability. We find that the failure of the respondent’s attorneys to contest removability at the hearing would not have affected the outcome of the case because the respondent is removable for having committed two crimes involving moral turpitude not arising out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct. Section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act. The Ninth Circuit has held that completed crimes committed on different dates or in different places are presumed to be separate and different crimes not arising out of a single scheme. See Leon-Hernandez v. INS, 926 F.2d 902 (9th Cir. 1991), citing Chanan Din Khan v. Barber, 253 F.2d 547, 548 (9th Cir. 1958). To rebut this presumption an alien must do more than simply present “any evidence” of a common scheme. Id. There must be evidence of a conscious, coherent plan or program of future action. Id. A vague, indeterminate expectation to repeat a prior criminal modus operandi is not enough to establish a single scheme. Id., adopting Nason v. INS, 394 F.2d 223 (2d Cir. 1968).
 
In this case, the respondent was convicted of two counts of prostitution in violation of Hawaii Revised Statute § 712-1200. According to the police report included with the respondent’s brief, the Honolulu Police Department had an ongoing undercover investigation of prostitution and other Morals related offenses at the respondent’s place of employment, Rainbow Toning & Oriental Relaxation Therapy. After offering two different undercover police officers sex for money on two separate days, the respondent was arrested and subsequently convicted. These facts raise the presumption that the crimes did not arise out of a single scheme and this presumption has not been rebutted. There is no evidence that the respondent’s offer of sex for money to two different individuals on two different days was part of a conscious, coherent plan or program of future action. Leon-Hernandez v. INS, supra. While the respondent’s convictions arose out of the single scheme of an ongoing undercover police investigation, her criminal misconduct leading to those convictions was no more than an indeterminate expectation that her customers would be willing to pay her for sex. Leon-Hernandez v. INS, supra. The respondent also argues that her criminal misconduct arose out of a single scheme because she was not given the mandatory sentence of 30 days imprisonment under Hawaii law for subsequent offenses. Respondent’s Brief at 20. We decline to accept this argument. The State’s decision on how to treat the respondent’s crimes for sentencing purposes does not affect the “single scheme” analysis established by the Ninth Circuit for immigration purposes. Accordingly, we find that the respondent is removable for having committed two crimes involving moral turpitude, not arising out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct and that the failure of her attorneys to contest removability did not affect the outcome of her case.
 
*4 We now consider the other bases for the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim. Failure to advise the respondent of the option to seek voluntary departure in lieu of a removal proceeding did not affect the outcome of the proceeding. The respondent does not allege that she would have opted for voluntary departure in lieu of a proceeding if it had been offered. Moreover, all of the evidence in the record shows that the respondent wanted to stay in the United States with her husband. This desire by the respondent suggests that her attorneys made a tactical decision to go forward with the proceeding in order to obtain a waiver of inadmissibility so that the respondent could remain in the United States. Tactical decisions, even if in hindsight unwise, do not constitute ineffective assistance. Thorsteinsson v. INS, 724 F.2d 1365, 1367-68 (9th Cir. 1984). Likewise, the premature decision to transfer the respondent’s file to her new attorney in Maryland does not constitute ineffective assistance. Although the respondent’s attorneys did not have the file for her merits hearing, they had already submitted many documents in support of her claim; both the Immigration Judge and the DHS attorney had these documents in their files. In addition, the Immigration Judge continued the hearing to give her attorneys a chance to retrieve the file. Tr. at 57-58. Finally, all of the documents submitted to support the respondent’s application for a discretionary 212(h) waiver were ultimately moot because she was statutorily ineligible for the waiver. See section 212(h) (stating that a lawful permanent resident cannot be granted a waiver if she has not lawfully resided in the United States for 7 years immediately preceding the initiation of removal proceedings).3 Accordingly, the foregoing actions by the respondent’s attorneys did not affect the outcome of the proceeding. We also find that the respondent was not deprived of a full and fair hearing due to an incompetent interpreter.
 
The interpreter’s errors cited on appeal are not significant. The respondent has not identified any examples of material testimony which was not translated, or explained how the Immigration Judge relied on incorrectly translated testimony in reaching her decision, or demonstrated how a better translation would have made a difference in the outcome of the hearing. Perez-Lastor v. INS, 208 F.3d 773 (9th Cir. 2000); Hartooni v. INS, 21 F.3d 336, 339-40 (9th Cir. 1994); Matter of G-, 20 I&N Dec. 764 (BIA 1993); Matter of K-L-, 20 I&N Dec. 654 (BIA 1993); Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105, 107-08 (BIA 1984)
 
The third issue we consider is whether section 212(h) violates the Equal Protection Clause of the Constitution. We have repeatedly held that we are not empowered to rule on the constitutionality of the statutes and regulations that we administer. See Matter of C-, 20 I&N Dec. 529, 532 (BIA 1992); Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335, 339 (BIA 1991); Matter of Fede, 20 I&N Dec. 35, 36 (BIA 1989); Matter of Valdovinos, 18 I&N Dec. 343, 345 (BIA 1982); Matter of Cenatice, 16 I&N Dec. 162, 166 (BIA 1977); Matter of L-, 4 I&N Dec. 556, 557 (BIA 1951).
 
*5 The final issue we consider is whether the Immigration Judge erred in denying the respondent voluntary departure. In order to be eligible for voluntary departure at the conclusion of proceedings the alien must show, inter alia, that she is, and has been, a person of good moral character for at least 5 years immediately preceding the alien’s application for voluntary departure. Section 240B(b)(1)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(1)(B). However, as defined, an alien cannot establish good moral character if she is a member of one of the classes of persons described in, inter alia, section 212(a)(2)(D) of the Act, Prostitution and Commercialized Vice. Section 101(f)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(f)(3). The respondent certainly fits within the description of section 212(a)(2)(D) of the Act as a person who has engaged in prostitution within 10 years of the date of application for a visa, admission, or adjustment of status.4 Therefore, by definition, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for voluntary departure and the Immigration Judge did not err in making this finding.
 
Based on the foregoing, we find that the DHS was not equitably estopped from initiating proceedings, the respondent received a full and fundamentally fair hearing, the respondent is removable for having committed two crimes involving moral turpitude not arising out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, we do not have the power to consider the constitutionality of section 212(h) of the Act, and the Immigration Judge did not err in denying her voluntary departure.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

3

2004 WL 848483 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BENEDICT BOY FLAHN
File: AXX XX5 465 - Orlando
February 26, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
K. Dean Kantaras, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On November 24, 2003, the respondent, through counsel, filed a motion characterized as a motion to reopen with this Board seeking further consideration of this Board’s September 24, 2003, decision based upon an allegation of ineffective assistance of prior counsel. To the extent that the respondent seeks reconsideration of this Board’s September 24, 2003, denial of his August 13, 2003, motion to reopen filed with the assistance of prior counsel, the respondent’s November 24, 2003, motion is properly characterized as a motion to reconsider. Any motion to reconsider this Board’s September 24, 2003, decision had to have been filed with this Board on or before October 24, 2003. The instant motion was not properly filed with this Board until November 24, 2003, and is an untimely motion to reconsider pursuant to the regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2).
 
To the extent that the instant motion seeks reopening, the final administrative order in the respondent’s removal proceeding was entered on February 12, 2003. Furthermore, the respondent has already sought reopening in this matter of August 13, 2003, in a motion that was denied by this Board on September 24, 2003. Thus, the instant motion is barred by both the time and number limitations on motions to reopen at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent argues that he is seeking reopening based upon the ineffective assistance of his prior counsel in support of his previous motion to reopen and that, therefore, the instant motion should be considered a timely motion to reopen his previous motion to reopen for further consideration. Even assuming such a motion is not barred by the number limitations on motion to reopen at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), the instant motion is unsupported by any evidence that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance in aiding the respondent in filing the prior motion to reopen. In this regard, the statements of respondent’s present counsel in the instant motion to reopen are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Moreover, the evidence submitted in connection with the instant motion is wholly insufficient to establish ineffective assistance of the respondent’s prior counsel. Indeed, the instant motion is unaccompanied by any material evidence relevant to whether respondent’s former counsel rendered ineffective assistance. Thus, the motion is unsupported by material evidence in support of the motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
Inasmuch as the instant motion fails to establish that the respondent’s prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance in assisting the respondent in filing his August 13, 2003, motion to reopen, we also find that the instant motion does not establish exceptional circumstances that warrant reopening or reconsideration of the respondent’s case on this Board’s own motion. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (The Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances).
 
*2 Accordingly, reconsideration of this Board’s September 24, 2003, decision is denied as untimely. Reopening of this Board’s February 12, 2003, decision is denied as untimely and barred by the number limitations on motions to reopen. Reopening of this Board’s September 24, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s previous motion to reopen is denied as barred by the number limitations on motions to reopen. Finally, this Board declines to reopen or reconsider the respondent’s case on our own motion because the instant motion does not establish exceptional circumstances that warrant reopening where the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is wholly unsupported by any evidence. The respondent and his counsel are cautioned not to further abuse the motions process by filing any additional motions to reopen that do not meet an exception to the time and number limitations on motions at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3).
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 848483 (BIA)
End of Document

2004 WL 848577 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NONNA S. SHAKRAMANYAN
File: AXX XX4 776 - San Francisco
February 25, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elisa C. Brasil, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kevin R. Lashus
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On May 15, 2001, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision, in which the judge denied her motion to reopen removal proceedings, which were conducted in absentia. The respondent filed a late motion to reopen on October 15, 2003. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen, filed with the Immigration Judge within 180 days, which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The Board is precluded from considering such a challenge to an in absentia order in the first instance. See Matter of Guzman, supra. The Board has jurisdiction to consider an appeal, when a motion to reopen an in absentia order is denied by an Immigration Judge. Id. As stated, we considered, and denied, the respondent’s appeal from such a motion in this case. The respondent now seeks to have the Board reconsider its decision, based on a claim that prior counsel was ineffective. However, as the DHS argues, DHS Opp., at 2-3, there is no good reason provided for why this motion was filed almost 2 1/2 years after we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent admits that she learned of our earlier decision no later than September, 2002 (Respondent Aff. at ¶¶ 7, 13; Respondent’s Mot., at p. 14). The respondent sent a letter to opposing counsel, stating that she intended to file a motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel, on January 24, 2003. She filed a complaint with the State Bar of California in February, 2003. The State Bar closed its investigation against former counsel, and declined to reconsider this decision on June 16, 2003. Current counsel was retained on June 24, 2003 (Respondent’s Mot., at p. 15). Nevertheless, the pending motion was not filed until October, 2003.
 
Equitable tolling of a filing deadline is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence on her own behalf. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir.2003). Moreover, given the respondent’s lack of diligence, this is not an exceptional situation in which we would exercise our sua sponte authority over an untimely motion. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997)(Board will exercise its sua sponte authority over an untimely motion only in an “exceptional situation”). See also Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373 (A.G. 2002); Ekimian v. INS, 303 F.3d 1153, 1156-60 (9th Cir. 2002) (acknowledging Board’s authority to reopen sua sponte is within its unfettered discretion).
 
*2 Accordingly, the pending motion is denied, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.

2004 WL 848557 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TRINIDAD ANTONIA RAMIREZ SALAZAR
File: AXX XX1 576 - Los Angeles
February 24, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on December 9, 2003, when we summarily affirmed without opinion an Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 2, 2002, finding her removable, as charged, but granting her the privilege of voluntary departure. On January 12, 2003, the respondent moved this Board to reconsider its decision alleging a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion is denied.
 
A motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration, be supported by pertinent case precedent and be predicated on a legal or factual defect in the most recent decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). In her motion, the respondent does not specify the legal or factual defect in our previous decision, and we find no error in our decision that would warrant reconsideration. Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is more properly characterized as a motion to reopen, because the respondent asserts new facts and makes new allegations. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). To the extent the respondent alleges ineffective assistance by former counsel, we note that in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), we laid out the requirements that the respondent must meet in order to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. First, the motion should be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent attesting to the relevant facts. Second, before the allegation is presented to the Board, the former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Any subsequent response from counsel, or report of counsel’s failure or refusal to respond should be submitted with the motion. Finally, if it is asserted that prior counsel’s handling of the case involved a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, the motion should reflect “whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not.” Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997). Here, the respondent has failed to comply with any of the established requirements to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Moreover, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that she suffered prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. We recognize that the present case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which has held that rigid adherence to the procedural requirements established in Lozada is not always necessary. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir 2000). However, where the respondent has failed to comply with any of the Lozada requirements and where the respondent has also failed to show any prejudice, we are compelled to deny the motion to reopen. See Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 848560 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELIZABETH BIRU
File: AXX XX5 092 - Arlington
February 24, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jim Tom Haynes, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On August 5, 2003, the respondent filed with this Board a motion to reopen and remand, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the motion. The unopposed motion will granted.
 
In Matter of Lozada, supra, this Board discussed the criteria which must be present for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel arising in immigration proceedings: (1) the alien must provide an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement entered into with counsel regarding the actions to be taken, and what representations counsel did or did not make in this regard; (2) counsel whose integrity or competence has been impugned must be informed of the allegations leveled against him or her, and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) the evidence of record must reflect whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not.
 
In this case, the respondent has complied with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent’s alleges that although she was eligible to adjust her status based on an approved I-130 visa petition, her prior counsel failed to file a motion to remand with the Board during the pendency of her appeal. Furthermore, the respondent’s former counsel did not inform her that her appeal was dismissed and that she had been granted voluntary departure by the Board on November 4, 2002. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board in order to pursue her claim for adjustment of status but the motion was denied by the Board on April 12, 2003 because of the respondent’s failure to comply with the Board’s prior order of voluntary departure. The respondent supports her motion with an affidavit from one former counsel admitting that mistakes were made in the respondent’s case and that Counsel’s failure to act caused the respondent’s grant of voluntary departure to expire, causing substantial prejudice to the respondent. The DHS has raised no objection to respondent’s motion and arguments.
 
Accordingly, we find that the respondent has demonstrated that her failure to depart pursuant to the terms of our November 4, 2002, order was due to exceptional circumstances beyond her control. See generally Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996) (stating that ineffective assistance of counsel could constitute an exceptional circumstance for an alien’s failure to appear for his hearing). We further find that the respondent has submitted sufficient evidence with her motion to establish prima facie eligibility for the relief she seeks in reopened proceedings, and to demonstrate that the previous procedural defects resulted from the ineffective assistance of prior counsel.
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, the respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
