2004 WL 1167362 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMON PORTILLO IZAZAGA1 ROSALINDA PORTILLO VALDOVINOS RAMON PORTILLO VALDOVINOS MARIA DE ROSARIO PORTILLO VALDOVINOS
Files: A75 261 564 - San Francisco
A75 261 566
A75 261 567
A75 261 569
January 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Emmanuel Enyinwa, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James S. Stolley, Jr.
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on April 29, 2002, at which time the Immigration Judge’s March 23, 2000, decision was summarily affirmed without opinion. On August 13, 2002, the respondents filed a motion to reopen. The respondents acknowledge that the motion was not filed in a timely fashion, but they urge that the time period for filing the motion should be equitably tolled because they received ineffective assistance of counsel who represented them before the Board.2 The Immigration and Naturalization Service (the “Service,” now the Department of Homeland Security) has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied as untimely.
 
A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date that decision was rendered, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2)(2002). In the instant case, the Board issued a decision in each of the respondents’ cases on April 29, 2002. A motion to reopen would therefore have been due on or before Monday July 29, 2002. As the respondents acknowledge, their motion was untimely where it was filed on August 13, 2002, approximately 2 weeks after it was due. The respondents, however, claim that the time period for the filing of their motion should be equitably tolled based on the reasoning of the decision of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit in Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). Specifically, they allege that they were unable to file a motion to reopen in a timely fashion due to the ineffective assistance of attorney David Neumeister, who represented them before the Board.
 
The respondents’ allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are unpersuasive. First, the respondents have failed to indicate how attorney Neumeister’s assistance was ineffective with regard to the filing of their motion. The lead respondent admits that attorney Neumeister provided him with notice of the decision issued by the Board, which the lead respondent received approximately 20 days after the decision was issued. See Motion to Reopen, attached Declaration of Ramon Portillo dated June 22, 2002 (“Declaration”). He also admits that attorney Neumeister gave him an appointment in July 2002, and, although he does not specify the date of that appointment in his declaration, the motion itself states it was “almost three months from the” date of the Board’s decision, thus indicating that the scheduled appointment was within the 90-day period for filing a motion to reopen. Motion to Reopen at 3, and attached Declaration. The respondents, however, chose not to keep that appointment, and sought other representation. The lead respondent admittedly met with new counsel approximately 5 weeks before a motion was due, yet failed to file a motion to reopen in a timely fashion. These facts alone in no way establish ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Neumeister with regard to the filing of a motion to reopen before the Board.
 
*2 In Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000), a case cited by the respondent to justify failing to comply with the requirements that the Board set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), the Ninth Circuit stated that “the Lozada requirements are generally reasonable, and under ordinary circumstances the BIA does not abuse its discretion when it denies a motion to remand or reopen based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel where the petitioner fails to meet the requirements of Lozada.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra, at 525. As discussed above, this case does not revolve “a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel” so as to excuse the respondents from satisfying the formal requirements of Lozada. Id at 525-26. The respondents have failed to set forth with particularity what their agreement was with attorney Neumeister with regard to any representation he would provide after issuance of the Board’s decision, to present any evidence that they informed attorney Neumeister of the allegations leveled against him or provided him with an opportunity to respond, or to indicate whether a complaint was filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. In light of the failure to comply with Lozada and the failure to present a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance, the respondents have not established that the time period for filing their motion to reopen should be equitably tolled under applicable Ninth Circuit precedent. Accordingly, the motion was filed out of time and will be denied as untimely.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

It is unclear whether the lead respondent intended to include his spouse, Rafaela Portillo Valdovinos, or his son, Ramon Portillo Valdovinos, in the motion to reopen, where he lists in the heading of the motion to reopen only the A-numbers of his three children and not that of his spouse. Nevertheless, in view of our decision in this matter, we need not determine whether his spouse should also have been included in the motion.

2

2004 WL 1167369 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN RAMOS
File: A73 479 251 - Newark
January 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Harlan G. York, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles Parker, Jr.
Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. An Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent on May 23, 2001. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38. The appeal was filed over a year late, on October 4, 2002.
 
The respondent urges the Board to accept the appeal out of time. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), in its brief, supports the decision of the Immigration Judge and takes no position on the untimely filing. The respondent claims that he trusted his former attorney, who represented him at the removal hearing, to file the appeal for him.1 However, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Xu Yong Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.2d 127, 132 (3d Cir. 2001)(Board’s requirements for making ineffective assistance of counsel claim are “a reasonable exercise of the Board’s discretion”). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, these cases require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. There is no convincing evidence that either former counsel was notified of the charges, or that a disciplinary complaint was made against former counsel. Rather, attached to the Notice of Appeal was respondent’s letter to the Essex County, New Jersey bar association, saying that he wished to bring a complaint against former counsel, and a letter from a Supreme Court of New Jersey arbitration committee, to the respondent, sending him information about their arbitration program. There is no evidence that a disciplinary complaint with the proper authorities was filed against former counsel. Moreover, while former counsel was provided with a copy of the arbitration committee materials, there is no convincing evidence that former counsel was notified of the charges against him.
 
We also note that the respondent concedes in the Notice of Appeal that he learned no appeal was filed when he was ordered to report for removal. Yet the respondent states in his affidavit that he was ordered to report for removal in May, 2002. Nevertheless, the appeal was not filed for 5 months. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 127, 134-135 (2d Cir. 2000) (party seeking to extend filing deadline based on theory of equitable tolling must show that he has acted with due diligence).
 
*2 We therefore find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.52, 1240.53, and 1241.31 (1999).

2004 WL 1167366 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZOYA PETROVNA MILENTYEVA
File: AXX XX2 834 - Los Angeles
January 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lea Greenberger, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening.
 
The respondent in this case was first ordered removed in absentia on June 5, 2001, but the proceedings were reopened on July 7, 2001, upon a motion by the respondent. She was then again ordered removed in absentia on March 6, 2002. Another motion to reopen was then denied on April 11, 2002. An additional motion was next denied by an Immigration Judge on August 29, 2002. It is the appeal from this last decision that is currently before us. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying her motion on August 29, 2002. The Immigration Judge considered the motion to be one for reconsideration and, as such, untimely. Further, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent had not presented sufficient evidence of ineffective assistance of counsel and that such ineffectiveness did not toll the time and number requirements of motions. She further found that the respondent failed to present sufficient evidence that any illness she may have suffered on March 6, 2002, was exceptional circumstances such that her in absentia order should be rescinded.
 
The respondent argues that the motion considered by the Immigration Judge in her August 29, 2002, decision was not one for reconsideration, but rather for reopening and was therefore not untimely. We do not find the various arguments in this regard as well as those concerning any ineffective assistance of counsel affect the outcome of this case.
 
The respondent was ordered removed under section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § .C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), which provides for a recission of the order only:
(i) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances (as defined in subsection (e)(1)), or
 
(ii) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that the alien did not receive notice in accordance with paragraph(1) or (2) of section 239(a) ¿8 U.S.C. § §1229(a)(1) or (2)º or the alien demonstrates that the alien was in Federal or State custody and the failure to appear was through no fault of the alien.
 
Section 240(b) of the Act. The Act defines exceptional circumstances as:
(1) Exceptional circumstances. The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances (such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.
 
*2 Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
Here, referring back to the March 6, 2002, hearing, the respondent claims she was unable to attend due to food poisoning. Whether or not she was inadequately served by counsel in the past does not alter the fact that, even in the current record, she has failed to provide sufficient evidence that her claimed illness was exceptional circumstances. The only evidence she has submitted in this regard is her statement that she was too ill to come to court, a letter from a doctor stating that the respondent told him she as too ill on the given day, and a prescription the respondent had previously received in Russia, unrelated to her claimed illness. While we stated in Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998), that a note from a doctor would help support a claim to serious illness, a note that is based entirely on self reporting by the respondent, rather than a medical professional’s first hand assessment, must be given less weight. While the respondent may well have been suffering from stomach problems on the appointed day, there is little support for her claim that her illness presented exceptional circumstances. The respondent has also failed to submit any affidavits from people who may have more contemporaneous knowledge of her illness. And, while not required to do so by statute, the respondent also failed to contact the Immigration Court on the day of her hearing, or even shortly thereafter to explain her absence. As we have noted, this further undercuts her claim. See Matter of J-P-, supra. Considering the high standard established by Congress for a showing of exceptional circumstances, we simply cannot find the totality of evidence the respondent has presented is sufficient to establish the existence of such in this case. See Matter of J-P-, supra; see also Celis-Castellano v. Ashcroft 298 F.3d 888 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
In closing we note that although the respondent appeared pro se before the Immigration Judge, she was clearly informed of the importance of attending the March 6, 2002, hearing and the consequences of her failure to appear (Tr. at 19). Based on the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANTANA-PEREZ, MARLEN
File: AXX XX4 615 - SAN ANTONIO
January 26, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dobbs, Dawn, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tilson, Allen Reid,
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated November 15, 2002, denying the respondent’s untimely motion to rescind an order entered in absentia on March 5, 2001, and to reopen the removal proceedings. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). See also sections 240(b)(5)(C) and 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I& N Dec. 181 (BIA 2001); Matter of Guzman, 22 I& N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999).
 
The respondent’s argument based on ineffective assistance of counsel is not supported as required under Matter of Lozada, 19 I& N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F. 2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Lara v. Trominski, 216 F. 3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000) (in agreement with requirements). Further, there is nothing in the record to indicate that the respondent entered into an attorney/client relationship with either Catholic Charities of Tennessee, Inc. or Attorney Evaristo Martinez before the in absentia order was entered. See Respondent’s Application for Adjustment of Status (Form I-693), dated July 4, 2001; see also electronic mail, dated December 4, 2002 (stating respondent met Mr. Martinez at a social gathering in March 2001 in Kentucky). Thus, the respondent’s argument fails.
 
The respondent claims she did not receive oral notice of the consequences of failing to appear at her removal hearing in her native language, Spanish. The record reflects, however, the respondent’s signature on the Notice to Appear (Form I-862) indicating that she was personally served the Notice to Appear on April 1, 1999, that she was provided a list of organizations and attorneys who provide free legal services to aliens, and that she was provided oral notice in the Spanish language of the time and place of her hearing and of the consequences of failure to appear as provided in section 240(b)(7) of the Act (Exh. 1). We are not persuaded by the respondent’s belated claim to the contrary not only because the respondent has not offered an affidavit or any other statement under penalty of perjury indicating that she did not sign the Notice to Appear voluntarily and of her own free will, but also because the respondent’s diligence in notifying the Immigration Court of her address changes, which is also discussed in the notice of the consequences of failing to appear at the hearing in the Notice to Appear, and her several pro se motions to change venue, belie the respondent’s claim that she did not understand the consequences of failing to appear at her removal hearing. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
